========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Mi du nece plu verba semani klari
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Wed, 3 Oct 2001 23:02:34 -0700 (PDT)

--- hzi{at}uol{dot}com{dot}br wrote:
> 	I will try to help you with my limited knowledge.
> 	Since my native language is a neo-romance (Portuguese)
I

Gratia!

> I have to say I was kind of amused to see the
questions
> that came up in your mind. Things I or other
latin-speakers
> would never have any doubt about.

I have several goals for my word list (which I will
describe more fully later). One of them is to avoid the
"accidental" multiple meanings inherited from the Romance
languages.

A perfect example is kanceri, for "crab" and "cancer". My
word list will NOT have one word mean both of those things.
(I'm leaning towards kanceri = crab, mali-amorfo =
cancer).

If I wanted a Romance IAL, I would choose LFN or Romanova.
One of Glosa's appeals (to me) is that it is slightly more
fair to non-Romance speakers.

> > bato;deep (?), depth (?);
>   This has to do with depth, as in a "bathysphere",
and

Ok.

> > bulbo;bulb (?);
>   Seed. A light bulb is somewhat seed-ahaped, is't it?

I've noted both meanings, but decided this word is too rare
to be in my core list.

> > decimali;decimal (?);
>   A "decimal" in Portuguese is a number with a decimal
> point; not a whole number and not expressed as a
fraction.

My current thinking: decimali should not be a Glosa word,
since it really should be dekamali. So, instead, I have:
deka-sistema for the decimal system, and mero for a
fraction. There should also be terms for integer,
real/non-integer (floating point), etc.

> > delega;delegate (to?)
>   You delegate someone the power to vote for you.

Ok.

> > dirigi;direct (?);
>   Guide or steer, or drive [as in car]. 

Ok. It won't be in my word list, because it is a dupe of
duce.

> > diskusi;conference (?)
> more like an international meeting. It is more formal,
and more
> important. It may also mean a "speech", or "lecture",
or
> "presentation", as in a medical congress, or an academic
lecture.

Hmmmmm. But diskusi sounds like discussion, which would be
a more informal conference. But there is also diskursi
which means discussion. This one is still open for me.

> > figura;figure (?);
>   It designates shape, no doubt about that. 

Ok.

> > fonda;background (?)
> > Is this like a visual backdrop? Or like foundational
> > background information?
> 
>   Both, depending on the context.

I might be able to live with this having two meanings.

> > hedo;fun (?)
> > ludo;fun (?)

Here's my current version:
hedo;fun (feeling)
ludo;fun (play)

> > idio;private (?);
>   It [this identidy]  doesn't belong
>   to anyone but yourself. Therefore, it's also
"private"

Ah. That helps. I've added:
idio;private (personal), personal

> > korka;cork (?);
>   Since there was no plastic around in Acient Rome or
> Greece, to make   things simple, I wouldn't make the
difference. 

Again here I've noted both definitions, and also marked the
word as being too rare for my core list. They didn't have
planes, cars, or computers back then either, but our
language must adapt to the times.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Mi du nece plu verba semani klari
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Wed, 3 Oct 2001 23:12:26 -0700 (PDT)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> My thoughts relating to Kevin Smith's questions.

Gratia!

> A word in Glosa is intended to stand for only one
> concept. But this concept may be represented in
> English by a large number of words, and remember 
> a Glosa word can act in any part of speech category.
> A metaphorical usage must also be allowed.

Understood. However, as much as possible I want each Glosa
word to stand for one clear concept, not several concepts
that are related in one culture. I also want each concept
to be represented by only one core word, so translators
don't have to make ambiguous choices.

I already responded to the other person who replied.
Fortunately, there was a fair amount of agreement between
the two of you. My notes below cover areas that my earlier
response didn't.

> > dirigi;direct (?);
> I have ~direkti~ as the preferred form. Both mean
> guide/steer.

My list has direkti as meaning "direction", which I took to
mean a compass point. Does it really mean to direct?

> > diskusi;conference (?)
> Any kind of discussion.
Which would make it redundant with diskursi.

> > imagi;image (?);
> A physical likeness.

Still not sure here. I need to think more.

> > kompani;company (?);
> Business.
> "Company come to visit" ~hospi~
> Company of soldiers would be ~grega~.

Very helpful.

> > koncentra;concentrate (?);
> Focus mental activity, time or resources on something.

Does fokali also refer to mental focus, or should it be
strictly physical? This is one where the abstract and
physical _seem_ closely aligned, although it may just be my
own cultural bias.

> > koncerne;concern (?);
> Worry is ~turba~. 
> Related-to is ~de~. 
> ~Gravi pro~ is to matter/be concerned about.

So should I just omit koncerne from my core list?

> > kontenta;content (?), constituent;
> Contents are ~plu in-ra~.
> Content is ~ge-place~ [pleased].
> Constituent is ~mero-ma~

So should I just omit kontenta from my core list?

> > limita;rather (?);
> Rather in the sense of "somewhat" / "to some extent or
> degree".

Ah. the british RAH-thur. I'll just leave that definition
out.

> > liqi;fuse (?);
> Yes, or fusing "apart" (melting) as an electrical fuse
> does.

I've included fuse (weld) and weld as defining words.

> > lisi;smooth (?);
> Yes, the G seems to have got separated from the rest of
> the word. ~Glisa~ is slip, slide. Smooth is ~glabro~.

Ok. Lisi does not mean smooth.

> > orange;orange
> Fruit

Agreed. Rubi-xanto is the color.

Kevin

> > > dirigi;direct (?);

Yes. 

I would use ~via~ for compass point direction.
 
> > > diskusi;conference (?)
> > Any kind of discussion.
> Which would make it redundant with diskursi.

Yes. They cover the same concept for me.
 
> > > imagi;image (?);
> > A physical likeness.
> 
> Still not sure here. I need to think more.

How about ~ikoni~, the Greek equivalent?
 
> > > koncentra;concentrate (?);
> > Focus mental activity, time or resources on something.
> 
> Does fokali also refer to mental focus, or should it be
> strictly physical? This is one where the abstract and
> physical _seem_ closely aligned, although it may just be my
> own cultural bias.

I would say that "focusing your mind" was a metaphor, 
and that "focusing your eyes, or your camera" was the
central concept here.

 
> > > koncerne;concern (?);
> > Worry is ~turba~. 
> > Related-to is ~de~. 
> > ~Gravi pro~ is to matter/be concerned about.
> 
> So should I just omit koncerne from my core list?

All the meanings seem to be covered. So it could be omitted .
 
> > > kontenta;content (?), constituent;
> > Contents are ~plu in-ra~.
> > Content is ~ge-place~ [pleased].
> > Constituent is ~mero-ma~
> 
> So should I just omit kontenta from my core list?

Doesn't appear to me to be a core concept.

Poli ami saluta,
Nick

"sodium hydroxide" du eqe a mixi ko aqa, i-ci sio es ma boni -- latini eqe 
pura, greke eqe mixi 
sid (Syd) [sId]

choice.

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 7 Oct 2001 21:18:08 -0700 (PDT)

In putting together my own personal Glosa core words list,
I am changing the following:

akti-verbi -> akti-verba
deskribe-verbi -> deskribe-verba
nima-verba -> nomina-verba.

"verbi" is not a core word, but "verba" is. "nima" is not
core, but "nomina" is. One of my (many) goals is
consistency, so hyphenated core words can only consist of
other core words. I strongly recommend the keepers of the
GID to adapt this same policy.

I plan to leave "poli" out of my core list, since it is
redundant with "multi". Given that, and the above policy, I
will be changing:

poli-pe -> multi-pe

As always, comments are welcome.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Plus plu qestio
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 7 Oct 2001 21:46:09 -0700 (PDT)

Mi du ergo de mi verba lista, e mi du habe multi questio.

As always, I would like to hear opinions from everyone on
these...

mite;send
How is this send different from bali?

morali;moral
Is this moral/good, or the concept of a moral which might
be relativistic? I assume the former (unfortunately) due to
the Western European culture, but want to be sure.

es la;exist
existe;exist
onto;exist
Is there any real difference between these? My current plan
is to remove "es la" and "onto", leaving just existe.

kontra;opposite
opositi;opposite
Is there any real difference between these? If not, I'll
probably leave out opositi and keep kontra.

petita;pet
Is this a noun or verb?

place;please
I plan to cast this as a request, and not tie it to the
concept of something being pleasing. I know that's where
the roots join, but I think they're separate concepts.

ponda;pound
Is this a pound of weight? If so, I'll drop it since we
have the metric system. Similarly if it's a pound of money
I'll drop it. I assume it's not the verb to pound.

primitive;primitive
I assume this refers to something being one-of-the-first.
Is it really different from proto? I'd like to drop it.

procesa;process
I assume this is both the noun (a method), and the verb (to
execute the method on something). 

promise;undertake
Most of the meanings of promise have to do with promises,
so this meaning doesn't make sense to me.

provide;foresee
Most of the meanings have to do with providing. How does
this relate to forseeing?

recepta;receipt
Is this the act of receiving? Or the piece of paper that
indicates it was received? It shouldn't mean both.

regula;principle
Most of the meanings have to do with regulating. Why is
principle here? It seems to be a side effect that regulate
-> rule (v) and rule (n) -> principle. I don't like it.

safe;manifest
I have no idea how this fits.

sensi;attitude
This meaning doesn't seem to belong

sibila;whistle, soothsay
Huh? I plan to make it whistle, and drop the soothsay
meaning.

sperma;pip
I thought a pip was a dot, like on a die. Sperma is seed.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 7 Oct 2001 21:57:38 -0700 (PDT)

--- Charles <charles{at}catty{dot}com> wrote:
> > In putting together my own personal Glosa core words
list,
> > I am changing the following:
> 
> Not objecting, but asking: Why? Is it a reform project?

I prefer to think of it as a clarification project, rather
than a reform project. Sometime soon, I'll write up all my
goals so it will become more clear. The end product will be
a Kevin Core Glosa list that I can use for my own writing
purposes. If anyone else chooses to use it, that will be
fine with me, but it's not my motivation.

Whenever possible, I am staying within the GID, and
preferably the GID core. In a very few cases, I will be
including some words in my core which are not in the GID
core. An example would be that there is no word in core for
"sink (n)", which seems pretty core to me.

I doubt I will make up any new Glosa words. I think I can
fill out my core list with words from GID basic, or by
hyphenating core words.

For my own sanity, I am greatly enhancing the english word
mappings to the Glosa words. In some cases this means
clarifying which English meaning was intended. In some
cases, it means adding more English words within the same
concept, so I can more easily find the Glosa word I want.
In some cases, it is omitting English words which don't fit
the "one" concept of that Glosa word.

Also for my own sanity, I plan to eliminate most or all
Glosa synonyms. Each of my core words will represent a
single concept, and will be the ONLY core word to represent
that concept. I am also leaving GID core words out of my
list for a variety of other reasons, each of which I will
document.

Note that I am not eliminating any words from Glosa. I am
merely creating a subset list that people can choose to use
if they wish--if they agree with my choices. I believe the
loose vocabulary of Glosa is its weakest point right now,
and this is my own personal solution.

As I said, it's mostly so I can create my own tool, and all
my aims and goals will be spelled out when I post the
list(s) on my website.

Thanks for asking :-)

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 8 Oct 2001 18:18:53 -0700 (PDT)

--- Marcel Springer <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de> wrote:
>  > I plan to leave "poli" out of my core list, since it
is
>  > redundant with "multi". 
> 
> The Glosa authors certainly prefered "poli".  It can be
> found more often in their publications. 

Interesting. On the positive side, "poli" seems to be more
Greek, and since Glosa hinges on Greek, I tend to favor
Greek roots when there's a choice.

However, my big concern about "poli" is confusion with
unrelated words like "polica", "polisi", and "politika". I
believe this confusion comes from the other Greek root of
Pol, roughly meaning person.

I do feel, somewhat strongly, that only one of the two
should be in any core list. At this point, I guess I still
lean towards keeping "multi" because it is more distinct.

Kevin

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, <kevins{at}pobox{dot}com>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Plus plu qestio
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 10 Oct 2001 00:46:43 +0100

Plus plu qestio de Kevin Smith 
>
> As always, I would like to hear opinions from everyone on
> these...
> 
> mite;send
> How is this send different from bali?

Same meaning in Glosa. One word is Latin and the other Greek.
I would prefer ~bali~.
 
> morali;moral
> Is this moral/good, or the concept of a moral which might
> be relativistic? I assume the former (unfortunately) due to
> the Western European culture, but want to be sure.

~morali~ is from ~more~ [custom]. So it's what's good
for you in your society/period of history.


> es la;exist
> existe;exist
> onto;exist
> Is there any real difference between these? My current plan
> is to remove "es la" and "onto", leaving just existe.

No difference. I prefer ~existe~.

> kontra;opposite
> opositi;opposite
> Is there any real difference between these? If not, I'll
> probably leave out opositi and keep kontra.

No difference. I would say ~anti~ also covers this meaning.

> petita;pet
> Is this a noun or verb?

Noun. I would use ~domi-zoa~.
 
> place;please
> I plan to cast this as a request, and not tie it to the
> concept of something being pleasing. I know that's where
> the roots join, but I think they're separate concepts.

~place~ seems only to be used as polite request marker.

~petitio~ ask/request.
~gluko~ pleasing/nice/comfortable.
~sukro~ sweet tasting.

 
> ponda;pound
> Is this a pound of weight? If so, I'll drop it since we
> have the metric system. Similarly if it's a pound of money
> I'll drop it. I assume it's not the verb to pound.

Money.

> primitive;primitive
> I assume this refers to something being one-of-the-first.
> Is it really different from proto? I'd like to drop it.

Yes. I'd prefer ~proto~ too.
 
> procesa;process
> I assume this is both the noun (a method), and the verb (to
> execute the method on something). 

Seems likely.

> promise;undertake
> Most of the meanings of promise have to do with promises,
> so this meaning doesn't make sense to me.

"undertake" can be "pledge to do something".
 
> provide;foresee
> Most of the meanings have to do with providing. How does
> this relate to forseeing?

Literal meaning. ~pro-vide~ seeing ahead. ~pro-~ = forward, is
an official "useful prefix", but I don't think they fit well into Glosa.

> recepta;receipt
> Is this the act of receiving? Or the piece of paper that
> indicates it was received? It shouldn't mean both.

~cepti~ is act of receiving.

> regula;principle
> Most of the meanings have to do with regulating. Why is
> principle here? It seems to be a side effect that regulate
> -> rule (v) and rule (n) -> principle. I don't like it.

~regula~ is rule/principle.
"regulate"/"control" should be ~krati~.


> safe;manifest
> I have no idea how this fits.

>From Greek saphes, clear, plain, manifest.
 
> sensi;attitude
> This meaning doesn't seem to belong
 
An "attitude" is a general feeling about something.

> sibila;whistle, soothsay
> Huh? I plan to make it whistle, and drop the soothsay
> meaning.

Latin sibilare, to hiss and Greek sybil "prophetess".
Can't have both words in Glosa.
 
> sperma;pip
> I thought a pip was a dot, like on a die. Sperma is seed.

Apple, orange and pear (etc) seeds are called pips.

Kevin Smith wrote
> --- Nick Hempshall wrote:
> > The more usual Glosa terms are
> > ~klavi-verba~
> > ~qualita-verba~
> 
> But neither of those are GID core (or basic). Akti-verbi
> and deskribe-verbi are.

True. But that could be a mistake!

> Klavi-verba doesn't make sense to me..."closed word"? "key
> word"? 

Key. The most important word in the sentence, and so
the key to its meaning.

> I also think "describing word" is somewhat better
> than "quality word".

That people's tastes differ, is one of the reasons that
Glosa has so many exact synonyms. The other reasons
being Greek and Latin sources, and the problem of having
to keep using the same words over and over when 
writing. The extra words don't  put a great strain
on the memory, as we all recognise many more words than 
we use. It seems that's the way memory works.

Poli saluta,
Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 9 Oct 2001 17:56:24 -0700 (PDT)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> > Klavi-verba doesn't make sense to me..."closed word"?
> > "key word"? 
> 
> Key. The most important word in the sentence, and so
> the key to its meaning.

Ah, but I don't think the concept of "key" as in unlocking
something (klavi) is really the same as "key" as in
important. So if you really mean the most important word,
it should be something like gravi-verba.

If you mean the word that unlocks the meaning of the
sentence, then klavi-verba would make sense. But that still
seems like a stretch to me.

Also, I'm not sure the noun really is the most important.
Some sentences don't have nouns, or only have objects and
not subjects. The distinction (to me) is still that a noun
names or identifies something. I guess idio-verba would
work for me, too.

But at this point, rather than trying to figure out a
better word for it, I'm inclined to go with the (corrected)
GID core word for noun: nomina-verba.

> > I also think "describing word" is somewhat better
> > than "quality word".
> 
> That people's tastes differ, is one of the reasons that
> Glosa has so many exact synonyms. The other reasons
> being Greek and Latin sources, and the problem of having
> to keep using the same words over and over when 
> writing. The extra words don't  put a great strain
> on the memory, as we all recognise many more words than 
> we use. It seems that's the way memory works.

True within reason. But I still want a core list that has
one word per meaning. Even if it's not my favorite.

Thanks for your input on this. I'm enjoying the discussion.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Plus plu qestio
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 9 Oct 2001 18:19:49 -0700 (PDT)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> Plus plu qestio de Kevin Smith 

Your response was very helpful, as always!

> ~morali~ is from ~more~ [custom]. So it's what's good
> for you in your society/period of history.

Makes sense. I trust that although the roots are similar,
that the words "more" (custom) and "morali" (morality)
should remain distinct concepts. I suppose "ne-morali"
would be amoral, and "no-morali" would be immoral.

> > kontra;opposite
> > opositi;opposite
> > Is there any real difference between these? If not,
> I'll
> > probably leave out opositi and keep kontra.
> 
> No difference. I would say ~anti~ also covers this
> meaning.

I wondered about that. Thinking further, "kontra" has
unfortunate similarity to "ko", given its opposite meaning.
My only concern about "anti" is that it has connotations of
hostility, whereas the others seem to be passive (or
objective?) states of opposite-ness. Perhaps "opositi" is
the right choice.

> > petita;pet
> > Is this a noun or verb?
> 
> Noun. I would use ~domi-zoa~.

I like that.

> > promise;undertake
> > Most of the meanings of promise have to do with
> promises,
> > so this meaning doesn't make sense to me.
> 
> "undertake" can be "pledge to do something".

Hmmm. To me, undertake is to actually begin to do
something. It goes well beyond a mere promise. I'll ponder
this more.

> > provide;foresee
> > Most of the meanings have to do with providing. How
> does
> > this relate to forseeing?
> 
> Literal meaning. ~pro-vide~ seeing ahead. ~pro-~ =
> forward, is
> an official "useful prefix", but I don't think they fit
> well into Glosa.

Then I suspect I'll drop this meaning.

> > recepta;receipt
> > Is this the act of receiving? Or the piece of paper
> that
> > indicates it was received? It shouldn't mean both.
> 
> ~cepti~ is act of receiving.

So "recepta" would be the confirming document. I'm not
thrilled that gluing "re" on the front turns it into a
document. But unless there's a cool hyphenated word that
means receive-confirm-document I'll probably leave it.

> > regula;principle
> > Most of the meanings have to do with regulating. Why is
> > principle here? It seems to be a side effect that
> regulate
> > -> rule (v) and rule (n) -> principle. I don't like it.
> 
> ~regula~ is rule/principle.
> "regulate"/"control" should be ~krati~.

Interesting. "Krati" currently seems to have strong
government overtones. We also have "sistema" for
organization. More thought required.

> > safe;manifest
> > I have no idea how this fits.
> 
> From Greek saphes, clear, plain, manifest.

Ok, manifest (adj - obvious), as opposed to manifest (v
-cause), or manifest (n - list of contents). Sounds like a
job for "klari".

> > sensi;attitude
> > This meaning doesn't seem to belong
>  
> An "attitude" is a general feeling about something.

Which really has little to do with the core concept of
sensing and perceiving (in my mind, at least). It's closer
to a belief ("kredi") or opinion ("doxo"). Not to mention
"mode" which currently lists attitude as a meaning.

> > sibila;whistle, soothsay
> > Huh? I plan to make it whistle, and drop the soothsay
> > meaning.
> 
> Latin sibilare, to hiss and Greek sybil "prophetess".
> Can't have both words in Glosa.

Right, and since whistles are much more common today than
soothseers (or prophets), I'm inclined to stick with
whistle. Unless there's a great Greek word for whistle we
could use...

Itera grati!

Kevin

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Plus plu qestio
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>

Nick pa grafo, 01-10-10:
 > Plus plu qestio de Kevin Smith 
 > >
 > > As always, I would like to hear opinions from everyone on 
 > > these...
 > > 
 > > mite;send
 > > How is this send different from bali?
 > 
 > Same meaning in Glosa. One word is Latin and the other Greek.
 > I would prefer ~bali~.

[ GID says: 
[ bali - cast (throw); fling; hurl; post (mail); send; throw;  
[          toss 
[ mite - send; transmit 

mite is from Latin mitto - to send, to throw. 
  Latin learners know the sentence a lot of chapters of Caesar's 
  "Bellum Gallicum" start with:  "Quibus rebus ductis legatos mittunt."
  (After this they sent diplomats.) 

ballo is from Greek ballo - to throw.
  (ballistics, ball, 
  devil=dia-bol-os= one throwing things into confusion). 

I think, we can use bali and mite as synonyms, but I would like 
the distinction to be perMITTEd 
  bali - (mainly) to throw; 
         to send (object is a thing that cannot move on its own) 
  mite - (mainly) to send (object is a person or a thing, delivered 
         by a person)

... and keep both in Core.  If only one, then bali is prefereable.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Charles <charles{at}catty{dot}com>
Date: Wed Oct 10 00:01:41 PDT 2001

I've often thought about what the "true essence" of Glosa
(or Interglossa) really might be, and attempted some "reform"
projects on a small test scale.

Hogben wanted to use Greek and scientific international roots.
I suppose this could be accomplished, but there are not many
such roots, and the vocabulary would be inadequate without
a more powerful derivational system than simple compounding.
Ron/Wendy used Latin/Romance to fill out the vocab in a highly
inconsistent manner. Unfortunately, so many roots conflict in
meaning between Latin and Greek that the result is confusion.

So, Glosa enthusiasts have been trying to clean up the vocab.
But without clear agreed principles, and lacking a charismatic
leader or significant financial investment, ...

POLI and PLU are both from the Greek POLY; MULTI is Latin.
A definitive list of all the articles/determiners is needed
before attempting to reform any one, because they form an
interdependent set. In fact, they are among the 100 or so
crucially important words of an "analytic" language. A true
vocab reform would gather the entire set of functional words
(particles) into a rational paradigm, and review them for
completeness and efficiency in actual use.

The "semantic" (rather than "grammatical") words are another
issue, and should be kept separate. Ron/Wendy did try to
categorize words, and I think their effort should be updated.
My own esthetic ideal would be to make the primary separation
obvious by reserving one-syllable words for particles and
doubles for semantic words, but that may not be achievable.


---------------------------------------------------------------------

For additional commands, e-mail: glosalist-help{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Plus plu qestio
From: Charles <charles{at}catty{dot}com>
Date: Wed Oct 10 12:32:03 PDT 2001

Quoth Marcel:

> ballo is from Greek ballo - to throw.
>   (ballistics, ball,
>   devil=dia-bol-os= one throwing things into confusion).

>   bali - (mainly) to throw;
>          to send (object is a thing that cannot move on its own)
>   mite - (mainly) to send (object is a person or a thing, delivered
>          by a person)

The Latin/Romance word for dance conflicts with the Greek for throw.
Using "ject = throw", we get many more unambiguous derivatives.
For a minimal core vocabulary, "bal" and such are too troublesome.

I'm thinking that a purely apriori vocab would be preferable
to a mangled naturalistic one such as Glosa and Esperanto.

But better still would be a careful selection of the most useful
non-ambiguous roots from Latin (and latinized Greek) without the
mangling, so that Europeans do get a head-start but for others
the roots are no worse than pure apriori (in other words,
avoid importing the many european irregularities).

Those unfamiliar with Lojban/Loglan should also examine the
list of "gismu" for hints as to core vocabulary selection:
http://www.lojban.org/files/roadmap.html#wordlists

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Changes from core
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 13 Oct 2001 16:42:32 +1000

At 23:23 10/10/2001 +0100, Nick Hempshall wrote:

  & I quote the post in full ...

>Kevin Smith wrote

>> --- Nick Hempshall <<nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:

>> > > Klavi-verba doesn't make sense to me..."closed word"?

>> > > "key word"? 

>> > 

>> > Key. The most important word in the sentence, and so

>> > the key to its meaning.

>> 

>> Ah, but I don't think the concept of "key" as in unlocking

>> something (klavi) is really the same as "key" as in

>> important. So if you really mean the most important word,

>> it should be something like gravi-verba.

>> 

>> If you mean the word that unlocks the meaning of the

>> sentence, then klavi-verba would make sense. But that still

>> seems like a stretch to me.

>> 

>> Also, I'm not sure the noun really is the most important.

>> Some sentences don't have nouns, or only have objects and

>> not subjects. The distinction (to me) is still that a noun

>> names or identifies something. I guess idio-verba would

>> work for me, too.

>> 

>> But at this point, rather than trying to figure out a

>> better word for it, I'm inclined to go with the (corrected)

>> GID core word for noun: nomina-verba.

>

>I should have explained that ~klavi-verba~ is Glosa for

>"verb".

>

>Yes. You are right - key doesn't equal important. And

>actually ~klavi~ used as a verb would be "to lock", so 

>even "unlocking the meaning"  cannot be the idea.

>

>Poli ami saluta,

>Nick

>

*  Friends, 

     For my part, I use ~nima-verbi~ to cover the NOUN concept, and 

             ~akti-verbi~ for the VERB idea.


     While the VERB might be the 'key word' in a sentence, defining the action, I am quite against the unnecessary use of metaphors in Glosa.


     This >concept-word<< element of Glosa's specification has the potential of being its most powerful feature, but, sadly, because we are all human .. and not analytical machines .. it could be Glosa's major downfall.  

     When my brother rattles off Esperanto at me, challenging me to reply in equivalent manner using Glosa, I cringe inwardly.  Esperanto is comfortable, and more or less translates into idiomatic form - as applies in most national languages.  But Glosa, because it holds out for ACTUAL meanings, not idioms and not metphors, works in a way that humans have not yet been taught to think.


     In old-world thinking, speaking .. by copying others .. is the natural thing to do, and has worked - up to now.

     Perhaps, in some future civilisation, people will learn to think first, then speak later: while for us - with our chilhood conditioning of using metaphors, cliches and idioms most of the time - speaking in Glosa can be painful, once we learn to *think in Glosa* the process of expressing ourselves using Glosa will become more automatic.


     Can we wait for civilisation to catch up with Glosa?


     On the base level of the lexicon, I would say the 'keys' and 'keyness' should be restricted to matters of opening and closing, and permissions to to enter.  Is a computer "key" something that gives you the 'permission to enter' a letter symbol?  Did John 'key' his letter into the computer, or did he 'press' it in?


   ~Jon pa kalvi in an grama, uti puta-me.~     [using the phrasal verb]

 ~Jon pa klavi an grama in u puta-me.~  [flows better & is better(?) Glosa]


         OR


  ~Jon pa presu an grama in u puta-me.~


         OR       


  ~Jon pa digita an grama in u puta-me.~


     Was it "fingered" or was it "pushed"?


     Ron Clark used to say "Play with the language."  My suspicion is that the Glosa-pe are not being 'playful' enough.


     The Golden Rule for Glosa seems to be: in seeking the answer to the question, #What am I really trying to say#?


     Do I seek the word that embodies the 'action' or the one that is the 'key' to the whole sentence.  Maybe this is too philosophical, or maybe, our old friend William of Occam can come to our assistance here.

     Cutting through to the essence of the concept, I would suggest most people would agree that 'action' is the keynote to the VERB concept, so ~akti-verbi~ could trigger the right associations in the minds of the majority of people.


     Similar reasoning would lead through NOUNS as 'names' to ~nima-verbi.~


     Of course, if we can get away with thinking words are labels, then we might seek the label that in the shortest number of letters, and with the simplest conceptual structure .. as well as being meaningful to the greatest number of people .. seems O.K.


     It is a bit of a juggle, three balls are in the air: wordshape, thought patterns and statistics.  Will the balls fall and make a mess of language, or will skillful communicators catch them all neatly - each time - to produce a string of serendipitous words?


Keep juggling,


Saluta,

  Robin

Subject: [glosalist] Active use language 
From: Kjell Rehnstrom <cellus{at}telia{dot}com>

Robin was discussing the question how to make it easier to speak Glosa. I think this is a matter of practice. I cannot imagine that Glosa should be so much more alien than many other languages, it is just the pedagogic method that is not good enough. People can learn to speak Latin. You begin with simple things like "Nauta sum", and then you go on building it up: Nauta suecicus
sum. In urbo habito...." And then you go on, learning more and more. Problem with Glosa is that you need a lot of motivation. And then one has to get over ones prejudices about the language....

Ethnic languages just are there, and there is nothing for you to change or to have an opinion about. With - say - English you cannot just starting reforming it if you stumble on something that you don't understand, if you see my point. And this is just what often will happen, when people learn an auxiliary language. They stop for good, or they start reforming it. But a few of the
learners struggle on and learn.

Cellus

Subject: [glosalist] Thinking in Glosa (was: Changes from core)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 13 Oct 2001 19:47:47 -0700 (PDT)

--- Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au> wrote:
>      When my brother rattles off Esperanto at me,
> challenging me to reply in equivalent manner using Glosa,
> I cringe inwardly.  Esperanto is comfortable, and more or
> less translates into idiomatic form - as applies in most
> national languages.  But Glosa, because it holds out for
> ACTUAL meanings, not idioms and not metphors, works in a
> way that humans have not yet been taught to think.

I'm not fluent enough with Glosa yet to be able to agree or
disagree. When translating, I do find myself constantly
trying to burrow through my idiomatic English to find out
what the heck I'm REALLY trying to say. 

However, when I'm on a roll, and am creating original
material directly in Glosa, the thought process doesn't
seem that radical to me.

If you are correct about thought patterns, then Glosa would
work relatively better for written communication, because
you have time to think it through, and can go back and
revise it.

>      On the base level of the lexicon, I would say the
> 'keys' and 'keyness' should be restricted to matters of
> opening and closing, and permissions to to enter.  Is a
> computer "key" something that gives you the 'permission
> to enter' a letter symbol?  Did John 'key' his letter
> into the computer, or did he 'press' it in?

Excellent question. Somehow the word for a a physical key
got recycled into a piano key, and then a computer keyboard
key. Even if we "press", or "finger" something in, we still
need a word for those little bits of plastic we're hitting.

Perhaps something like litera-butona, or litera-ru. (By the
way, I'm using litera because grama (I thought) meant the
kind of letter you would drop in a mailbox, and not a
single alphabetic character). Assuming this is the case, I
plan to promote litera from basic up to core in my own
lists.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Active use language 
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 13 Oct 2001 19:59:36 -0700 (PDT)

--- Kjell Rehnstrom <cellus{at}telia{dot}com> wrote:
> Ethnic languages just are there, and there is nothing for
> you to change or to have an opinion about. With - say -
> English you cannot just starting reforming it if you
> stumble on something that you don't understand, if you
> see my point. And this is just what often will happen,
> when people learn an auxiliary language. They stop for
> good, or they start reforming it. But a few of the
> learners struggle on and learn.

Excellent point.

My own approach to Glosa follows my approach to writing
computer programs, which is closely aligned with "Extreme
Programming", or XP. It is highly iterative, where you get
a skeletal version of something out there quickly (and with
high quality within the parts you release), and rely on
feedback to direct future development.

So for Glosa, I'm perfectly willing to accept the existing
core as a starting point. It's "good enough" for me...sort
of. I want to fix what I see as the errors (e.g. nima-verbi
-> nomina-verba), and strip out any parts that don't seem
consistent (high quality).

>From that starting point, I hope the language can grow
incrementally and organically. Individuals will, in their
own writing, coin new words. Glosa-pe will either accept
and adopt those words, or will reject them (implicitly, by
not using them). The language will grow and evolve
organically.

Assuming this organic growth is possible, I don't want to
do a lot of reform up front. I won't worry too much about
whether the core represents exactly the right set of
concepts or not, or whether this particular Latin root
might be better than that Greek one. Those issues can
resolve themselves in the long run.

For now, I want to get Glosa to a point where is is usable,
and then use it. And I want as many people as possible to
start using it, because that's the ONLY way we'll know if
it's a viable IAL or not. In my mind, most reform projects
are doomed because they are too dramatic, when incremental
would be more effective.

If we don't have a small community of people communicating
regularly in Glosa within a few months (?), I will most
likely lose interest and move on to some other IAL. Glosa
is my first choice, because of its unique syntax. But I'm
not here for a pure hobby. I want something that will
actually generate results in the world.

Glosa doesn't have to be the most popular IAL for me to be
happy. It just needs to be used for actual communcation by
a growing community of people.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Bali e mite (pa: Plus plu qestio)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 13 Oct 2001 20:08:26 -0700 (PDT)

--- Marcel Springer <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de> wrote:
> I think, we can use bali and mite as synonyms, but I
> would like 
> the distinction to be perMITTEd 
>   bali - (mainly) to throw; 
>          to send (object is a thing that cannot move on
> its own) 
>   mite - (mainly) to send (object is a person or a thing,
> delivered 
>          by a person)
> 
> ... and keep both in Core.  If only one, then bali is
> prefereable. 

Hmmm. It seems to me that "throw" and "send" really are
different concepts. However, rather than the distinction
you suggest, I would propose that bali would be to
physically toss or throw something. Mite would be to send
or transmit something. So:

Mi pa bali u paketa trans u kamera; a me fe-sibi
Mi pa mite u paketa trans u urba; a mi fe-sibi.

Mi pa bali u bola a Jo.
Mi pa mite u nota a Jo.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] A priori vs. mangled naturalistic (was: Plus plu qestio)
From: Kjell Rehnstrom <cellus{at}telia{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 14 Oct 2001 08:33:49 +0200

Kevin Smith wrote:

> --- Charles <charles{at}catty{dot}com> wrote:
> > I'm thinking that a purely apriori vocab would be
> preferable
> > to a mangled naturalistic one such as Glosa and
> Esperanto.
>
> Not for me.

Li prekondition e ke li apprendero ha un forti motivation. In un kase de
ti sorte il e mult possibli usar un apriori vocabularie. In kase ke no ha
motivation, on debe operar kon maximali komprensiblia (komprensiblitate).

On debe in addition agnoskar (prender a gnosis, a kognoscentie) ke li
apprioria kuba (jace, demora, lies) in li ocules del observator. Li
apprioria e un tekniki term e no un behavioristici term. Sole li lingvist
posse determinar si un term e appriori o no.

> > But better still would be a careful selection of the most
> > useful non-ambiguous roots from Latin (and latinized
> Greek)
> > without the mangling, so that Europeans do get a
> head-start
> > but for others the roots are no worse than pure apriori
> (in
> > Jother words, avoid importing the many european
> irregularities).
>
> Yes, with reservations about the Latin/Greek part. More on
> this in another message.

Si li ling no ha un didaktiki (educational, teaching) kapacitia il no
posse ganiar, nam il debe kontender kon li majori mundiali lings.

I wrote this in my own auxiliary idiolect in order to show what I wanted
to say rather than describing it in abstract forms that I am not capable
of getting across in English, I'm afraid. So I beg your pardon for not
having written in Glosa, as I know I should.

Cellus

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Thinking in Glosa (was: Changes from core)
From: Kjell Rehnstrom <cellus{at}telia{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 14 Oct 2001 09:01:07 +0200

Kevin Smith wrote:

> I'm not fluent enough with Glosa yet to be able to agree or
> disagree. When translating, I do find myself constantly
> trying to burrow through my idiomatic English to find out
> what the heck I'm REALLY trying to say.
>
> However, when I'm on a roll, and am creating original
> material directly in Glosa, the thought process doesn't
> seem that radical to me.

What you do when you learn a language is that you train your brain to go
from machine code to a speak and write program. So when you are trying to
translate back and forth between Glosa and English you're just stopping
yourself. When I am trying to write something in English I most certainly
don't translate it into English from Swedish, I just write it, because I
have the thoughts there and I try to express them. I may need a word and
then I look it up. The thought may be there, but it has a Swedish word
for it, provided I cannot find the English one. It is somewhat like a
multilingual wordprocessor I have. When I try to use its foreign language
fonts on my Swedish keyboard it shows the "international" letters and
substitutes the typically Swedish ones with, say, Russian! I think that
is how our brains work, as well.

So when we learn a language we should train our brains much as we do when
training a dog. We teach them to behave.

As the Glosa promotors have not thought about a didactic way of teaching
the language, I think we ourselves have to find the method.

But Glosa has the problem as any auxiliary language will have. The
learner will question whether the language is good or not.

For your amusement I could tell you a little story from my school time.
My English teacher told me that English was a world language. And I asked
her why then it had such a crazy spelling, you couldn't learn it. German
was plain and simple in comparison. Whereon she replied that "English is
nothing like these stupid language Ido or Esperanto...." (As a matter of
fact I was learning Esperanto at the time and found that much easier than
English).

I should - in order to avoiding misunderstandings - point out that I am
not, nowadays, a proponent of Esperanto. It doesn't answer my criteria
for an auxiliary language. So I am not secretly propagating it.

> If you are correct about thought patterns, then Glosa would
> work relatively better for written communication, because
> you have time to think it through, and can go back and
> revise it.

Provided that the inventors of Glosa really can speak it fluently, even
others must be able to do so.

> >      On the base level of the lexicon, I would say the
> > 'keys' and 'keyness' should be restricted to matters of
> > opening and closing, and permissions to to enter.  Is a
> > computer "key" something that gives you the 'permission
> > to enter' a letter symbol?  Did John 'key' his letter
> > into the computer, or did he 'press' it in?
>
> Excellent question. Somehow the word for a a physical key
> got recycled into a piano key, and then a computer keyboard
> key. Even if we "press", or "finger" something in, we still
> need a word for those little bits of plastic we're hitting.

Any language will generalize where other languages particularize. You can
select _clav, tast, tangent_ for the key that you press. An English-Greek
vocabulary would give inspiration I think.

> Perhaps something like litera-butona, or litera-ru. (By the
> way, I'm using litera because grama (I thought) meant the
> kind of letter you would drop in a mailbox, and not a
> single alphabetic character). Assuming this is the case, I
> plan to promote litera from basic up to core in my own
> lists.

Isn't _grama_ the product of something _"graf"-ed_? We have _phonogram_ a
_phonograph_ was a machine as it were "writing sounds". Then an e-mail
could be an "elektronografie" or "elektronogram" (made into Glosa
according to the rules necessary). The parts of those words do exist in
all European languages, as far as I know, even in Finnish. (Icelandic
being the sole possible exception). On the other hand an Icelandic
learner has studied English and has therefore learnt the International
vocabulary and has been taught what the international morphemes mean. So
international Greek might well be a mor concrete thing to him than it is
to us.

Cellus

Am I to understand that as:

> Jon pa grama in puta-me.  [John typed-his-letter in(to) a computer.]

John lettered in a computer.

> Jon pa grama per puta-me. [John wrote-his-letter by-means-of (on) a
> computer.]

John lettered by a (via) a computer (the computer)

> Jon pa puta-me an grama. [John used-a-computer-to-bring-into-existence his
> letter.]

John computered his letter.

Is _an_ a possessive, like _his_?

If so this is a typical English usage. We had to learn that particularly that
when we waved the hand, put the money in the pocket and when he sent the
letter then the English would wave their hands, put their money in their
pockets, and they would send their letters!

The use of posessives is slowly creeping into Swedish as people read so much
English over here.

A funny thing is that we can say: "They shook on the head!" when English
speakers would say "They shook their heads!"

So as you can imagine I don't give much for Whorfff or what his name was...

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Posessives and shaking heads (was: Changes from core)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 14 Oct 2001 12:11:47 -0700 (PDT)

--- Kjell Rehnstrom <cellus{at}telia{dot}com> wrote:
> Am I to understand that as:
> 
> > Jon pa grama in puta-me.  [John typed-his-letter in(to)
> a computer.]
> 
> John lettered in a computer.

Right. Or John lettered into a computer.

> > Jon pa grama per puta-me. [John wrote-his-letter
> by-means-of (on) a
> > computer.]
> 
> John lettered by a (via) a computer (the computer)

Right.

> > Jon pa puta-me an grama. [John
> used-a-computer-to-bring-into-existence his
> > letter.]
> 
> John computered his letter.

Exactly.

> Is _an_ a possessive, like _his_?

An means he, him, or his, depending on its position. 

An du sedi. He is sitting.
An feli du sedi. His cat is sitting.
Mi pa sedi epi an. I sat on him.

Did I miss any?

> The use of posessives is slowly creeping into Swedish as
> people read so much English over here.

How does Swedish handle the posessive case without
posessives? Just curious.

> A funny thing is that we can say: "They shook on the
> head!" when English speakers would say "They shook their
heads!"

In Glosa, I would probably say:

Mu pa osci mu kefa. They shook their heads.

This would be legal, but doesn't make as much sense to me:

Mu kefa pa ge-osci. Their heads shook (got shaken).

The latter sounds more like someone riding a roller
coaster.

Of course, shaking heads is a cultural thing. I worked with
some folks from India who nodded "yes" by rocking their
head side-to-side (moving each ear toward the closest
shoulder). It took me a while to distinguish this motion
from the US gesture for "no" which is swiveling the face
back and forth, keeping the nose level.

So one of these would be clearer:

Mu pa indika "ne" per osci mu kefa.
Mu pa indika "ja" per osci mu kefa.
Mu pa indika "no-lauda" per osci mu kefa.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Postal stuff and keyboards (was: Thinking in Glosa)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 14 Oct 2001 15:23:24 -0700 (PDT)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> Yes. ~Litera~ is letter (A.B.C.) and ~gram~ is letter
> (post/mail).

So posta would be the mail system, and grama would be a
mail item. We also have nota, which would serve for letter.
Do we really need all these terms? If so, what are the
distinctions? 

I think grama is already too close to other meanings--after
all, Glosa has gram for weight and gramatika for grammar.
Thus, I suggest:

posta = mail (collective), mail (v), mail (system)
posta-ra = individual mail item of any kind--letter,
parcel, postcard, etc.
nota = note, letter (mail)
litera = letter (a,b,c)
grama gets dropped out of core
grama-ve becomes posta-ve = envelope

> ~Ru~ (tool) seems to grand a term for a computer keyboard
key.

Tools can be pretty simple, so I don't mind ru...

> How about ~digi-ra~, which parallels ~manu-ra~ (a
handle)?

This also sounds fine to me. I assume this would be any
kind of button or finger-lever. It could be confused with
thimble, ring, or false fingernail, but I can live with
that.

But a keyboard becomes digi-ra-fa, which sounds a bit odd.
Maybe digi-ra-mensa or digi-ra-taba? Or in-digi-ra
(input-finger-thing)? Of those, I like digi-ra-fa best,
even though it is strange.

Kevin

Yes, but kratera is any bowl or basin. Over here on the
west US coast, we rarely call it a basin or wash basin.
It's a sink (especially in the kitchen). So it took me a
while to even make the connection to kratera.

Mi du uti u kratera pro vora mo.
Mi du uti u lava-kratera pro lava.

On a related topic, I'm guessing that lava-ka would be best
for loo/washroom/bathroom/water closet/head, but I'm still
trying to figure out the word for toilet. The best I have
thought of so far is merda-ru.

Kevin

> --- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> > Yes. ~Litera~ is letter (A.B.C.) and ~gram~ is letter
> > (post/mail).
>
Kevin Smith grafo
> So posta would be the mail system, and grama would be a
> mail item. We also have nota, which would serve for letter.
> Do we really need all these terms? If so, what are the
> distinctions?

~Nota~ seems to mean peice of paper with writing/printing on it.
So "bill" (money and restuarant check), "notice" (poster/sign/ad),
or written "record" of an event. A letter would fit into this category.

~Posta~ would seem to be letters in transit or at the start or end of their
journey.

> I think grama is already too close to other meanings--after
> all, Glosa has gram for weight and gramatika for grammar.
> Thus, I suggest:
>
> posta = mail (collective), mail (v), mail (system)
> posta-ra = individual mail item of any kind--letter,
> parcel, postcard, etc.
> nota = note, letter (mail)
> litera = letter (a,b,c)
> grama gets dropped out of core
> grama-ve becomes posta-ve = envelope

Yes. Makes sense, if ~nota~ is not too general a term.

> > ~Ru~ (tool) seems to grand a term for a computer keyboard
> > key.
>
> Tools can be pretty simple, so I don't mind ru...
>
> > How about ~digi-ra~, which parallels ~manu-ra~ (a
> > handle)?
>
> This also sounds fine to me. I assume this would be any
> kind of button or finger-lever. It could be confused with
> thimble, ring, or false fingernail, but I can live with
> that.

Thimble = ~alexi-digi~
Ring = ~zona~
False fingernail = ~falsi ungu~

> But a keyboard becomes digi-ra-fa, which sounds a bit odd.
> Maybe digi-ra-mensa or digi-ra-taba? Or in-digi-ra
> (input-finger-thing)? Of those, I like digi-ra-fa best,
> even though it is strange.

Yes. I prefer ~digi-ra-fa~ too.

Saluta,
Nick

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] A priori vs. mangled naturalistic
From: Charles <charles{at}catty{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 14 Oct 2001 22:58:01 -0700

Cellus wrote:

> Li intention esset just ke ego no volet advocar un partikulari ling ke
> jam e cognoscet. Ego supponet (supposet) ke ego konserva li obktivia
> usando mi propri auxiliari idiolekt, tanto long ke id e komprenditi.

I understood your statement above, though I read it at approx
half-speed compared to English. This is all that can be expected
of an auxiliary language. I have had enough experience in a very
similar dialect (Occidental) to be convinced of this, but:

The one great potential strength I imagine in Glosa is that
it would permit a simple and direct one-to-one translation of
morphemes. This is due to its "analytical syntax-based grammar".
In particular, it should be easy to go from Chinese or English
to Glosa, using only a 2000 (or so) word list. A web interface
can make this less painful -- e.g., my old experimental page:
http://catty.com/~catty/lang/dict.cgi

It seems that most adults cannot learn an inflectional language,
so Esperanto by its basic design is absolutely impossible as an IAL.
Glosa removes almost all the morphology and approximates a pidgin,
which is the precisely correct typology for an adult 2nd language.

Edgar De Wahl designed Occidental to be easily "evolve-able"
in the analytic direction. Its verbal system is similar to Glosa
in relying on pre-postioned auxiliaries. A similar pattern formed
the basis of the Lingua Franca. But EDW was unable to solve the
problem of derivation *without* suffixes. I don't think Glosa
has succeeded there (derivation) either.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Sinks and bowls (was: Changes from core)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 15 Oct 2001 16:51:45 -0700 (PDT)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> ~Soma-va~ is the bath and ~balno~ is to bathe.

Veri. Sed pan bi pa ge-nota ko (1) e (G), seqe; in u GID
verba lista. Boni alo mali, mi du kredi; u GID kordi (++) e
GID basi (+); du es plu maxi nu e oficia arki. Ni de plu-la
verba pa ge-inklude, pro uno tende.

Pan-tem, mi ne du tenta dina u "korekti" Glosa. Sed, mi du
tenta trovi u solidi e sati mero; mi du pote uti.

Balno ne sio ge-turba mi, sed id du mero semani homo de
lava.

Mi anti-amo soma-va. Id du feno ma homo de "coffin" a mi,
alo posi "sleeping bag". Plus ne-exakti.

Kevin

===
True, but they are noted with (1) and (G) respectively, in
the GID word list. For better or worse, I'm treating the
GID core (++) and GID basic (+) as being the most
up-to-date official sources, and neither of those made the
cut, for whatever reason.

As always, I'm not trying to dictate what is "correct"
Glosa, but rather am trying to find a solid and adequate
subset that I can use reliably.

Balno wouldn't bother me, other than having some
overlapping meaning with lava.

I don't like soma-va at all. It seems more like a coffin,
or perhaps a sleeping bag. Too vague.

Kevin

Subject: [glosalist] Anti, kontra, no, non, e opositi
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 15 Oct 2001 17:27:27 -0700 (PDT)

Mi pa puta de plu-ci verba: anti, kontra, no, e opositi.
Pan-ci du 
semani pene iso de alelo. 

Mi nuli-kron amo 'no', kausa id es plus proxi a 'ne'. U GID
du 
ero inter 'no' e 'ne', mi kredi:

ne foto: Posi debi no-foto

no-dina: Debi ne-dina
no-funktio: Debi ne-funktio
no-kine: Debi ne-kine
no-profunda: Probabili debi ne-profunda
no-resista: Debi ne-resista
no-sati: In plu kaso, debi ne-sati
no-semani: In plu kaso, debi ne-semani
no-soni: Debi ne-soni
no-stimula: Debi ne-stimula
no-sura: Posi debi ne-sura
no-vide: Debi ne-vide

Tende evita konfusi, mi du tende uti 'anti-' vice 'no-'. Id
du 
es mega klari, e radi de Greek.

'Kontra' du es ne-boni, kausa id du es homo de 'ko'.
'Opositi' es 
meso-boni-mali, sed id ne du habe uno profito vice 'anti'.

Ko-co, mi du skema; fu apo 'kontra', 'no', 'non', e
'opositi' de 
mi Glosa kordi lista.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Fosi: Dig or pour
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 15 Oct 2001 17:37:30 -0700 (PDT)

Akorda a GID, 'fosi' du semani ~dig~ alo ~pour~. Sed plu-la
semani ne homo. Qo semani fu debi resta, e qo fu debi muta?

~Dig~ posi es 'spata', alo 'uti spata', alo 'spata-akti'.
~Pour~ posi es 'lase flu', alo 'sti flu'.

Kausa ~fossil~ du es kognati a ~dig~ in Latin, mi sio
ma-amo; fosi du semani ~dig~. Qe, u-ci du boni semani?

Kevin

===
According to GID, 'fosi' means dig or pour. But those
meanings are dissimilar. Which meaning should remain, and
which should change?

Dig could be 'spata', or 'uti spata', or 'spata-akti'.
Pour could be 'lase flu', or 'sti flu'.

Because fossil is related to dig in Latin, I would prefer
that 'fosi' means dig. Does this make sense?

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, kevins{at}pobox{dot}com
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Anti, kontra, no, non, e opositi
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Mon, 15 Oct 2001 20:59:06 -0400 (EDT)

On Mon, 15 Oct 2001, Kevin Smith wrote (excerpt):

> Mi nuli-kron amo 'no', kausa id es plus proxi a 'ne'. U GID
> du ero inter 'no' e 'ne', mi kredi:

    When I compiled the original Glosa Basic Reference which I
put up on the www with Clark&Ashby's permission, following some
correspondence, the intention was to distinguish between
opposition and simple negation.  They are not the same thing,
and some languages (nat and con) do not always distinguish them
well.  'ne' was for simple negation and 'no-' for opposition.

-- 
Paul Bartlett
bartlett{at}smart{dot}net
PGP key info (superseded address) in message headers

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Anti, kontra, no, non, e opositi
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 15 Oct 2001 18:08:17 -0700 (PDT)

--- "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net> wrote:
> On Mon, 15 Oct 2001, Kevin Smith wrote (excerpt):
> 
> > Mi nuli-kron amo 'no', kausa id es plus proxi a 'ne'. U
> GID
> > du ero inter 'no' e 'ne', mi kredi:
> 
> the intention was to distinguish between opposition and
simple 
> negation.  They are not the same thing,

Clearly. However, I believe in several cases (see my
earlier email), the wrong one was chosen. 

Perhaps the most clear case, in my mind, is 'no-kine'
(motionless). It's not the opposite of motion; rather, it's
a lack of motion. Therefore, it should be 'ne-', and not
'no-'. 

The others range from obviously (to me) incorrect, to
arguably incorrect, to those which could reasonably be
'ne-' or 'no-', depending on the intended meaning.

Kevin

From: "Alexander E. Kirpichev" <brickswall{at}mtu-net.ru>

Plu mi karo Glosa-pe,
Mi ne habe multi tempo, sed mi vo dice  vi; mi pa (e nu du) face u programa
pro plu puta-me (sistema Windows) de Glosa. Qe vi vo ski; u programa face
qo?
Vide:

mi =I, me, my    ne =not    habe =possess
multi =much    tem =during    sed =but
mi =I, me, my    vo =want    dice =speak
vi =you(plural)    mi =I, me, my    pa =did, =-ed
e =and    nu =present    du =continue: =-ing
face =make    programa =program    pro =in favor of
plu =the, more than one    puta-me =computer    de =relating to
glosa =language    qe =question(start)    vi =you(plural)
vo =want    ski =know    programa =program
face =make    qo? =what?


Mi puta u-ci programa fu es " u programa pro plu internatio komunika " e id
fu difusi Glosa in u munda.

mi =I, me, my    puta =think    u-ci =this( =the =here)
programa =program    fu =shall    es =be(to be)
u =the, an, one thing (not plural)    programa =program
pro =in favor of    plu =the, more than one    komunika =communicate
e =and    id =it    fu =shall    difusi =spread    glosa =language
in =in    u =the, an, one thing (not plural)
munda =world

Poli ami saluta,
Alex.
poli =many
ami =friend
saluta =greet

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Kevin, GID, Core, Similar Synonyms 
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Thu, 18 Oct 2001 12:35:00 +0200

Karo Kevin e plu hetero pe, 

I am glad about Kevin's activities to make a new Core dictionary! 
The discussion in Glosalist he has stimulated and the contributions of 
Nick and others are very interesting!  This all should be evaluated 
and the results should be built into the GID. 

You may know, that I once made a list of "Similar Synonyms".  It has 
about 400 items.  I have sent it to Wendy Ashby and got suggestions 
from her, which words are prefereable and which should be eliminated. 

This list must be built into the GID necessarily, too.  (If not, IMO 
Glosa has disqualified itself from beeing a serious attempt for an 
IAL.)  Unfortunately this is a very tedious work, that needs a bit 
concentration.  So I am very slow with it.  When it will be ready, it 
will have changed also the Core-dictionary of today. 

Because I do not want to hinder Kevin's work with my slowness, 
or that our parallel working results in bad contradictions and 
disappointment, I decided to update the GID as far as it is now 
  ( http://www.glosa.org/gid/index.html , hope, files are ok.).

The "Similar Synonyms"-list is on 
  http://www.glosa.org/gid/simsyn.htm . 
New: now there are also Wendy's suggestions written, as far as they 
are considered, and the changes to the GID they caused. 

Wendy's suggestions are on 14 pages, 4 of them are considered.  The 
other 10 are scanned now and are temporarily on a website.  Links to 
these GIF-files (about 70 kB each) are also in the simsyn.htm ! 

Everybody, who wants to play with the Glosa words, may visit the 
simsyn.htm file, please. 

Saluta - Marcel

*  Boni ergo Alexander.

   Tu programa dona bi mode de lekto u mesage: plu-pe fu tosto etekti pro u
direkti lekto de Glosa, vice cerka dia plu translati.

   Pro u persona; qi logi England-lingua, u-ci metode lice an, alo fe, de
salta ex verbi a verbi intra u traduce sektio; seqe, mu pote kursi longi u
solo Glosa sektio .. tem tena plu semani de plu verbi, intra mu menta.

   U-ci certa dona u fo rapidi metode pro plu-pe de gaino koragio - ko u
lekto de Glosa. 

   Posi u tali programa pote gene ge-face pro plu hetero natio lingua.

   U problema acide kron u neo-pe tenta grafo mu-auto Glosa.  Posi, u-ci
sistema pote gene ge-uti te dona plu gradu idea de u sintaxi de Glosa: per
uti u ge-limita verbi-lista, na pote dona u pusi info - ko plu translati ex
Alexander programa; seqe, na pote dona qestio koncerne plu idea intra
plu-ci verbi - expekta plu responde uti simi forma iso plu exempla. 

  Nece, u-ci mode de doci Glosa habe u persona; qi lekto e korekti plu
eforti de plu gene-sko-pe.  Alterno, na pote dona mo, alo bi, exempla de
plu korekti responde, a plu qestio.

  Itera, boni ergo Alexander!

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Fosi: Dig or pour
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Fri, 19 Oct 2001 21:06:19 +1000

At 23:19 16/10/2001 +0100, Kevin wrote:
>Kevin Smith grafo
>
>> Akorda a GID, 'fosi' du semani ~dig~ alo ~pour~. Sed plu-la
>> semani ne homo. Qo semani fu debi resta, e qo fu debi muta?
>> 
>> ~Dig~ posi es 'spata', alo 'uti spata', alo 'spata-akti'.
>> ~Pour~ posi es 'lase flu', alo 'sti flu'.
>>    .....
>> ===
>> According to GID, 'fosi' means dig or pour. But those
>> meanings are dissimilar. Which meaning should remain, and
>> which should change?
>> 
>> Dig could be 'spata', or 'uti spata', or 'spata-akti'.
>> Pour could be 'lase flu', or 'sti flu'.
>> 
>> Because fossil is related to dig in Latin, I would prefer
>> that 'fosi' means dig. Does this make sense?
>> 
>
>This confusion seems to be due to a typo in one 
>of the sources documents for the GID.
>~Fosi~ is to dig, and ~fusi~ is to pour.
>
>Nick
>
*   Es veri so.
    Ex uno ero ko mo litera na pote gene u tali konfusi.

    _Glosa 6000_ habe  (E - G) pour  =   fusi

    Anti-co, per lase mo liqi kade in u hetero liqi, na gene u sensi de mo
liqi fusi in u hetero [Eng "infuse"].
    Sed mi logi ke u-ci uti eqa u meta-fero [metaphor]: u Latino verbi
``fusus`` eqa 'ge-fusi epi u surfacia'; tem un idea de kausa uno metali de
muta ex solidi a liqi esce, in England-lingua, "to fuse"; anti-co, u-ci
idea in Latino eqa ``liquefacio``.  
    Posi, ~fusi~ [=to pour] sio habe un auxi-verbi iso ~in~, ~epi~ alo
~kade~ te dona klari semani:-
       An fusi-in u lati.
       Fe fusi-epi u sola u hidra.  =  Fe fusi u hidra epi u sola.
       Id pa fusi-kade an vesti.

    Alterno, Kevin uti: ~lase-flu~ este fo boni, pluso:-
       Jon pa lase-flu u xeno liqi in u fluvi.

    E so, qe, na pote dice: ~U gina pa fusi bi tasa de tea~?* 
                          [The woman poured two cups of tea.]

    Alo, qe, na nece uti u sintaxi: ~U gina pa fusi (u) tea in bi tasa.~
                                    [The woman poured tea into two cups.]

    Nu-co, mi este u simpli  "pour" = ~fusi~ dona sufici pro na 
neo Glosa-pe.

Saluta,
  Robin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] More word list work
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 09:08:31 -0700 (PDT)

While shifting several no-xxx to be ne-xxx, I came across
'no-semani' (meaningless). This seems to confuse two or
three definitions of the English word 'meaning':
1) dictionary meaning
2) philosophical purpose
3) conceptual consistency

Thus, I propose that nonsense, senseless, and meaningless
would more accurately be captured by 'ne-logika'
(illogical).

---

The recent GID changes (thanks!) did include some
unfortunate choices. The one that really jumped out at me
is: 'manu-pe' (manager). This arises from the confusion
between two meanings of handle:
1) physically manipulate something (with your hands)
2) manage, take care of, control

The two meanings are not closely related, and should not be
covered by the same Glosa word.

To me, 'manu-pe' sounds like a hand-model (someone who is
paid to photograph their hands for advertisements). I
definitely won't use this to mean manager. 

Management is different from leadership ('duce'). It's more
related to control. So perhaps 'regula-pe' would be
reasonable for manager.

---

'Tekno' has bothered me for a while. It covers both
technology, and skill/cleverness. And, if you include
'no-tekno' (clumsy) it also seems to cover physical
dexterity. I believe its meaning should be limited to
technology.

So we need other words for cleverness. Possible candidates:
'intelige', 'strategi'. Or perhaps 'vafro' from the basic
list. Or maybe 'versuto'? Are there others?

We also need physical dexterity. Probably 'agili' from the
basic list?

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Word list issue: dina
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 09:25:41 -0700 (PDT)

'Dina' means both power (n), and compel (v). To me, these
are quite different, and should not be covered by the same
word.

Since dynamic, dynamo, and dynamite all refer to the power
(n) meaning, it seems like that's the one that should stay.

So we need a word for compel, coerce, oblige, force (v),
enforce. We have 'koerce' (coerce) in the basic list, or
'domina' (dominate). Or perhaps 'uti-dina' (use force). I
think 'koerce' is the best fit.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Kevin, GID, Core, Similar Synonyms 
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 10:55:30 -0700 (PDT)

--- Marcel Springer <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de> wrote:
> You may know, that I once made a list of "Similar
> Synonyms".  It has about 400 items.  I have sent it to
Wendy 
> Ashby and got suggestions  from her, which words are 
> prefereable and which should be eliminated. 

Seeing her original text is quite helpful. Whether I agree
with her or not, it gives me more insight into the
intentions and process.

> This list must be built into the GID necessarily, too. 
> (If not, IMO Glosa has disqualified itself from beeing a
serious
> attempt for an IAL.)  

I'm not quite sure what you mean by this. I guess you mean
that the results need to be integrated into the GID, which
I agree with.

> Because I do not want to hinder Kevin's work with my
> slowness, or that our parallel working results in bad
> contradictions and disappointment, I decided to update 
> the GID as far as it is now 

Thank you very much for this. My list has taken an
interesting journey, and it has a ways to go. I started
with the Glosa Basic Reference (GBR), not realizing that
GID core would have been a better starting point.

After working with that list some, I went through and
compared every word with GID core, adding GID words, and
noting words that were non-core. I have been working with
my list for quite a while, and will continue to do so. Of
course, I'll use the updated GID now that it is available.

I will probably publish an interim version of my list soon,
in case anyone wants to see the work in progress.

Later, I'll make another complete pass of comparing my list
with GID core, making sure I didn't miss anything, and
seeing how much I have really changed. At that point, I
might decide to publish my work as a list of changes from
GID-core, but I suspect not (because it would be quite
difficult).

It is a lot of work, but it is generally fun and rewarding.
Of course, one of my goals is to shrink the core list,
which means someone who works with core in the future will
have a smaller job.

Kevin

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] deko e gru
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 11:52:30 -0700 (PDT)

I'm a bit confused by the recent synonym changes to deko
and gru.

First, 'decent' was removed. I can see 'descent' being
removed, but to me 'decent' fits right in with 'seemly' and
'proper'.

Second, I'm trying to distinguish between 'deko' and 'gru'.
To me, 'deko' is related to decorum--a values judgement on
proper-ness and suitability. Contrast that with 'gru',
which to me is a physical or logical (objective) concept of
suitability.

Does that sound reasonable, or am I missing something?

Kevin

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Still more questions...
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 13:39:58 -0700 (PDT)

I'm trying not to ask questions here that can be answered
using Wendy's notes. [Note, however, that I can't actually
read the GIF files on my computer/browser. I'll try it on
my wife's machine.]

I assume 'organi' is like a bodypart organ, and not like a
musical organ?

I suspect 'radi' should just mean the root of a plant. We
have 'basi' as a conceptual root of something (plus 'origi'
for an origin). Perhaps radi isn't needed in core at all?

Is 'relatio' the generic word for any kind of relation,
relationship, or relative?

Does 'spaci' mean outer space (as opposed to vacant space)?

'Spekula' is listed for both mirror and speculate. Mirror
should probably be something like 'reflekti-ra' (promoting
'reflekti' from basic to core). 

Speculate could then remain 'spekula', except that it seems
reasonably close to 'divina'. Is there a significant
difference in meanings between 'divina' and 'spekula'?

Kevin

vice u xilo ergo-ka pa-loko. U xilo-ergo-arti pa existe de

puta; u-ci akti du representa plu muta de nu-tem.

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Ceiling and floor
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 20:16:26 -0700 (PDT)

Although they seem like pretty fundamental concepts, GID
core/basic doesn't really do well with either floor or
ceiling.

Floor is 'pedi-lo' (foot-place). While this does work, it
seems pretty vague and cumbersome for something so basic.
I'm trying to find a word for platform (other than the
obviously imported "plataforma'). 
I had hoped to construct 'raised-floor', but 'leva-pedi-lo'
could be either 'raised-foot place', or 'raised
foot-place'. I suppose it could be 'leva pedi-lo', but I
would like to use a fully-hyphenated word, since it's a
complete concept.

Meanwhile, our word for ceiling ('tegu') is also lid or
cover. This leads to the extremely odd and jarring 'pedi-lo
tegu' (literally foot-place cover, but which could be seen
as 'foot-place ceiling') for carpet or rug. 

At a minimum, I would suggest using '-te' for carpets and
rugs...pedi-lo-te if we're stuck with 'pedi-lo'.

And I think the concept of ceiling is important enough to
warrant its own core word, apart from lid or roof.
Hmmm..perhaps 'sub-stego' or 'intra-stego' would be good
enough. I'd sure like something better.

Kevin

From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>

~titula~ is 'title'. I assume that would be a label, such
as the title of a book or the title of a person. Not a

covered either by ~norma~ and/or ~mode~. Any thoughts?

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Tena has too many meanings
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 20:56:50 -0700 (PDT)

~Tena~ currently has, as far as I can tell, three disparate
meanings:

1. hold, contain, keep, preserve, retain, stock (store),
conserve
2. grip, clutch, restrain
3. support, sustain, uphold

Actually, 'restrain' might be its own meaning, and perhaps
conserve might be its own as well. Perhaps grip and clutch
could be in with hold, but I see one concept of storage,
and another of posession.

I could see using ~manu~ for grip and clutch, exept that
one of the meanings of ~manu~ is currently 'hand over'. It
doesn't seem right to have one word have meanings similar
to both hold and release at the same time. 

So I guess I would see:
~stora~ contain, preserve, stock (store)
~tena~ hold, grip, grasp, clutch, retain, keep (retain)
~sofi-uti~ conserve
~???~ support, sustain, uphold

Help?

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ceiling and floor
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 20 Oct 2001 21:05:39 -0700 (PDT)

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> Many have said that the Glosa vocab needs some revision.
> What criteria besides personal preference can be invoked?
> Without a system, it remains as disorganized as it began.
> Reliance on a user community evolving it has worked
> (to some degree) for Esperanto, but Glosa does not have
> 100,000 users and 100 years to evolve credibility.

An interesting way to put it.

If the ten (?) active people on this list voice their
preferences, then we can use a consensus to move forward.
Otherwise, it will be the editor of a given list who will
have to make their own (personal) decisions. At the moment,
for my list, that's me.

Glosa lacks a single, visible, authority figure. Actually,
having such a figure can be both a blessing and a curse.
Without that, I am trying to stick within what I understand
to be the intentions of Ron, Wendy, and y'all who have
worked on the GID.

Really, I don't see the disorganization as being a terrible
problem. I would expect Glosa (or any language) to become
less organized over time, with use. So we just have a
headstart :-)

As I said before, I would like to solve the problems
incrementally, rather than as a sweeping reform. In some
ways it is inefficient, but in others it is more efficient
(it's certainly easier and less painful). 

The incremental approach may not lead to the holy grail of
a perfect vocabulary, but then I don't think a perfect
vocabulary would remain so for very long, anyway. 

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ceiling and floor
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 21 Oct 2001 08:25:38 -0700 (PDT)

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> > The incremental approach may not lead to the holy grail
> > of a perfect vocabulary, but then I don't think a
perfect
> > vocabulary would remain so for very long, anyway.
> 
> If not, why bother with an IAL? Or why not use one of
> the neo-Latin IALs such as Interlingue? 

For me, Glosa's strongest appeal by far is its isolating
sentence structure. I don't like conjugations, and I like
the idea (whether true or not) that Glosa might be easier
for a Chinese person to learn than a neo-Latin IAL. There
are a lot of Chinese people in the world.

The vocabulary is secondary (to me). Learning a foreign
vocabulary will always be challenging. To whatever degree
the words can remind us of concepts by tapping into
commonly-used scientific roots, that's a plus. Beyond that,
it's more like a priori, where one of the main goals is
avoiding false friends.

I don't think Glosa's current vocabulary is substantially
worse than Esperanto's. Every language has flaws.

However, you are correct that if we can't build a small
community of people who care about Glosa and actually use
it, the language will not grow.

Kevin

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Glosa and Dutton SpeedWords
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 21 Oct 2001 23:16:07 -0700 (PDT)

I ran across mentions of Dutton SpeedWords as an IAL, and
noticed some similarities to Glosa. What really got my
attention was their list of 500 root words. I am still
struggling with the size of the Glosa core list, so I
thought I might learn something by looking at a small list.

It turns out the list is not bad, but certainly not large
enough for me to consider it as a good list of core
concepts. Dutton relies heavily on affixes to generate more
words, and the results are not represented in this list.

As an exercise, I mapped each of their roots to a Glosa
word. The result is available on my website, in a temporary
location (so please don't create any permanent links to it)
here: 
http://qualitycode.com/temp/duttondict.txt

Kevin

Cute! You might consider expanding that to include

the well-known "essential" English word lists,
as we discussed here a year or so back.

of highest-frequency English words; 93% of all English
vocabulary by actual usage is covered by just 6000 roots.
You would need to list them anyway for your own use,
especially for semi-automated translation.
