To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Mon, 22 Oct 2001 20:19:42 -0700 (PDT)

I was recently asked why I use "du" so much in my Glosa
writing. My response was that it's the only tool I know of
to indicate where the subject stops and the verb starts. 

As English speakers, we can usually figure out which word
is the first verb-like word in the sentence, but
technically, I believe it is ambiguous.

Take this example from the Glosa Basic Reference:
Na nece sti logi u demo.   

This could mean "Our need begins to understand a people",
or "Our requirement's beginning understands people". Rather
than the intended "We must make people understand."

So I would write "Na du nece sti logi u demo" or more
likely in this case: "Na fu nece sti logi u demo". 

Am I missing something?

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 25 Oct 2001 12:14:10 +1000

At 21:36 22/10/2001 -0700, Charles pa responde:

>> I was recently asked why I use "du" so much in my Glosa

>> writing. My response was that it's the only tool I know of

>> to indicate where the subject stops and the verb starts.

>

>You do need to use auxiliaries (or tense particles or something)

>before verbs, and articles (or determiners or prepositions)

>before objects, because like you said.

>

*   Saluta Mi plu amika,

    Quickly, in English, I would say that ~du~ is functional {continuous tense}, and not a necessary as a VERB PHRASE 'flag'.


    IF ... and that is a big word ... if the glosa is well (thought out and) written, then the start of the 'action' will be clear.


    Simply, there is a difference between,


      ~U kanis fago u sito.~    AND    ~U kanis du fago u sito.~

     [the dog eats the food]          [the dog is eating the food]

                                      [...  continues to eat  ...]


    I believe that harder than locating the start of the VP can often be defining the VP's end.  This difficulty occurs with 'phrasal verbs' in English:   The boy ran over the road.   The car ran over the boy.


   Who, or what, is "over"?  When is it a verb aauxiliary, and when is it a preposition?  There are even more outrageous constructions in English, but I haven't time to think them up.


   In Glosa we do have AUXILIARIES which are the bits you add after the 'verb', or VERBOID,  E.G.  ki in;   kade ex;   dice supra.


         ~Un alti, koleri gina celero ki in u kamera.~ 

        [the tall angry woman quickly moves into the room]


                             | VERB PHRASE |

         The tall angry woman goes quickly into the room.  .... 1.


    OR is ~ki in~  =  enter  ?    ... ~in~  being a verb auxiliary ???


        [the tall angry woman quickly enters the room]

                      

                             | VERB PHRASE  |

         The tall angry woman quickly enters the room.   ...... 2.


If there is a problem, and there seems to be one, is it an English language problem or a Glosa language one?


Is the meaning of the Glosa phrase much the same, either way?  OR  Do we need different Glosa renditions of the two resulting English sentences (1. & 2.), when we attempt to translate them back to Glosa?


My belief is that it is an English language problem (error/fault), and not a Glosa problem. 


N.B. In the Glosa sentence, the 'qualifier', ~celero~, is the 'flag' indicating the start of the VERB PHRASE.


N.N.B. In the first two Glosa sentences, there is a clear, function-word 'flag', ~du~, at the start of the second VP; but, with the first sentence, the VP comprises only one word, the substantive, ~fago~, and this word must be taken as  the 'flag' for the start of that phrase.


 Rule 1 in Glosa syntax is the NP-VP-NP, Subject-Verb-Object Rule.  Any self-respecting Glosa word finding itself sandwiched between two NOUN PHRASES has to acknowlege that it has the position of, and must have the function of, a VERB PHRASE.


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

 P.S. I might do as Kevin suggests, and write in Glosa, but we are having a very difficult Federal election over here - what with war hysteria and the deepening recession/depression - and I am standing as the "No GST" candidate for my area.  So, time is at a premium.  Also, with the way that our conservative government is changing into a right-wing dictatorship, we mightn't need elections in four year's time .. after martial law is imposed.  So, this might be our last chance to fight for democracy, and I fear that we will lose!    R.G.

*  Forcing people to understand things is a difficult concept at the best of times .. look at education, advertising, propaganda and politics.  Maybe I contradict the basic reference, here, but I would push the words together into a clearly defined concept structure, by sticking in a hyphen.  Perhaps Ron Clark forgot to put it in, or as usual, he thought people were more intelligent than they are, and that they could read his mind.


   Needless to say, in context, and in the heat of the political debate, EVERYONE would know what was implied by ~Na nece sti logi u demo.~

  But it is  bad example for learners trying to learn Glosa - cold - without context. 


   Actually, though, ~nece~ could not be much other than a VERBOID, here.  However, without the hyphen to bind the words into one concept, your reading of ~nece~ as a 'noun' could be correct.

 

   [We needs must make understand the people]

   [Our need: to make understand the people]

   

~Na nece: sti-logi u demo.~"Our need (is to) make the people understand"

                           "We need (to) make the people understand." 


      The trick I use here is adding some punctuation: true, in speech, we cannot see the punctuation, but we can hear the vocal inflections.


>This could mean "Our need begins to understand a people",

>or "Our requirement's beginning understands people". Rather

>than the intended "We must make people understand."

>

*  I'd say   "requirement"  =  ~nece habe~


>So I would write "Na du nece sti logi u demo" or more

>likely in this case: "Na fu nece sti logi u demo". 

>

>Am I missing something?

>

>Kevin

>

*   Keep interspersing the tense particles as VP 'flags' until you find more fluent ways of using Glosa.  Have you got access to much written Glosa?  Now I have a scanner, I could send you pages from old copies of _Plu Glosa Nota_. 


    The difficult cases seldom come up in polite letter-writing, but there needs to be some rule for all cases, because, with Glosa, *we are all learners.*


    What you are missing is the somewhat elusive ''feel'' of Glosa: it is not boringly prescribed, but excitingly poetic.  However ther is a sort of magic portal you go through to feel at ease with the language.


    I must try to find time to write in Glosa, as well.


Saluta, 

Robin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu, 25 Oct 2001 11:15:17 -0700

>> You do need to use auxiliaries (or tense particles or something)
>> before verbs, and articles (or determiners or prepositions)
>> before objects, because like you said.

> Quickly, in English, I would say that ~du~ is functional {continuous tense},
> and not a necessary as a VERB PHRASE 'flag'.

It is functioning as a tense/aspect marker, and because Glosa
places that sort of particle at the beginning of a verb phrase,
it also serves to flag where the preceding noun phrase terminated.
In other languages, such particles are agglutinated to the verb.

> IF ... and that is a big word ... if the glosa is well
> (thought out and) written, then the start of the 'action'
> will be clear.

Just running together the major words without particles
results in confusing "headline English" that can be parsed
only with the help of context and ESP. We need to make
at least one "grammatical" part-of-speech division, to
distinguish between the particles and content-words.

> I believe that harder than locating the start of the VP
> can often be defining the VP's end.  This difficulty occurs
> with 'phrasal verbs' in English:
> The boy ran over the road.   The car ran over the boy.

Yes, that's another big problem. Some people (Romans) might try
to solve it by preposing and attaching the adverb to the verb.
If many people ever try to use Glosa, some will do likewise.

> If there is a problem, and there seems to be one, is it
> an English language problem or a Glosa language one?

Yes! Importing more English into Glosa seems inadvisable.
I've written simple language parsers and can attest to the
difficulty of locating phrase boundaries when those little
"redundant" particles are elided. What makes a language easy
to learn and use is a clear separation (and definition) for
the 100 or so essential grammatical function morphemes.

And the authors of Glosa apparently realized this, because
they have separated the vocabulary into (somewhat ideosyncratic)
categories. They have not taken the final step of explaining
that every noun phrase begins with a quantifier or preposition,
and a verb phrase requires an initial TAM or voice particle.
But that's the way it makes sense and can actually work.
(Oversimplifying just a little.)

> I am standing as the "No GST" candidate for my area.
> So, time is at a premium.

Good luck. But having dabbled in politics a bit here, I prefer
to solve human problems with technology. A good IAL on the net
would be worth many election victories.

--- Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au> wrote:
>     IF ... and that is a big word ... if the glosa is
> well (thought out and) written, then the start of the
> 'action' will be clear.
> 
>  ~Un alti, koleri gina celero ki in u kamera.~ 
>  [the tall angry woman quickly moves into the room]

Would this be "moves within the room" or "moves into the
room"? I would have expected it to be the former.

> N.B. In the Glosa sentence, the 'qualifier', ~celero~, is
> the 'flag' indicating the start of the VERB PHRASE.

But couldn't this be "The tall angry feminine speed(thing)
moves in the room"? Or "The tall(thing) angrily and
femininely moves in the room"? I know these particular
words seem unambiguous, but would that be true of all words
in this sequence?

I keep trying to come up with an example, and haven't
really found a good one. Why? It seems that although Glosa
words can theoretically be used as noun/verb/adj/adv, most
of them really can't. Most words are really locked into one
or two parts of speech, by their definition.

I'll keep thinking about this.

> democracy, and I fear that we will lose!    R.G.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu, 25 Oct 2001 11:37:21 -0700

> It seems that although Glosa
> words can theoretically be used as noun/verb/adj/adv, most
> of them really can't. Most words are really locked into one
> or two parts of speech, by their definition.

The traditional parts of speech are not respected by
modern linguists, nor by generations of unhappy students.
But some division into rational word classes is necessary,
and makes parsing (and reading) less intractable.

The original Interglossa defined a small set of "verboids"
intended to constitute the entire set of verbs -- a radical
concept that could be rehabilitated in the form of "light verb
constructions". Ogden did something similar in Basic English.

Instead of "Bob kicks the dog", "Bob gives a kick to the dog".
Eliminating the heavy verbs can solve many difficulties!

    I myself think that this was one of the strong points about
Interglossa.  With the set of verbal words small and fixed, and their
their meaning developed by "amplifiers," the problem of recognizing 
verb phrase boundaries was much reduced.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 2001 13:23:33 +1000

At 11:37 25/10/2001 -0700, Charles grafo:

>> It seems that although Glosa

>> words can theoretically be used as noun/verb/adj/adv, most

>> of them really can't. Most words are really locked into one

>> or two parts of speech, by their definition.

>

>The traditional parts of speech are not respected by

>modern linguists, nor by generations of unhappy students.

>But some division into rational word classes is necessary,

>and makes parsing (and reading) less intractable.

>

>The original Interglossa defined a small set of "verboids"

>intended to constitute the entire set of verbs -- a radical

>concept that could be rehabilitated in the form of "light verb

>constructions". Ogden did something similar in Basic English.

>

>Instead of "Bob kicks the dog", "Bob gives a kick to the dog".

>Eliminating the heavy verbs can solve many difficulties!

>

*   Ron Clark pa dice: ali verbi pote habe u funktio de holo plu mero de voka.  U-ci ge-dice-ra habe supra-simpli.


    Kron mi tenta forma u _Deskribe Gramatika_ (epi mi Reti-lo), ex diskursi ko plura hetero-pe, mi pa detekti u pusi numera de plu funktio, e simi pro verbi kategori.


    Na detekti u major grega; qi habe no-limita, de concepa pro plu ra, plu akti, e plu deskribe.  Anti holo plu verbi de u-ci grega habe un abili de funktio iso holo tri kategori, generali plu verbi de u-ci SUBSTANTIA [substantives] grega pa habe u ge-asocia koncepa.  Freqe plu-ci 'substantia' habe solo mo posi uti: te deskribe solo ra, alo akti alo u deskribe.  Sed pro u maxi numera de mu, per metafero, alo ka mu natura,  plu-ci verbi habe u ge-asocia koncepa; qi pote gene ge-uti fo lati, 

   EG  u kursi  .  te kursi  .  u kursi persona  .  u kursi-pe  .  kursi


   EG  u kanis  .  u kani-iso kompani-pe  .  u kanis-triko  .

         te kanis mi plu pedi-grada  (idioma)


   EG  u kloro kapita-ve  .  te sporta epi u kloro  .  na face-kloro un agri per uti fito-sito  .  an pa jugo u kloro-grega


    U ma pusi kategori de pli verbi es plu FUNKTIO VERBI; qi habe nuli ge-asocia koncepa; tu pote vide, alo toka .. sed, iso plu signa e plu braketa de matematika don a tu u posi de ludo ko plu numera, plu funktio verbi lice tu ludo ko plu koncepa.

    Nece, itera iso matematika, plu vario funktio verbi habe difere mode de akti.  Plu 'tem-verbi' habe loka pre plu AKTI VERBI; plu 'pre-posito' introduce u PUSI-NIMA-GREGA [NOUN PHRASE]; plu qestio-verbi introduce plu questio; plu 'negativi-verbi' don u opositi semani ad u verbi alo frasa; etc.


    --------

    Koncerne un analisi de ge-grafo lingua, mi logi: id habe mega signifi.  Plu eduka-pe nece ski u struktura e funktio de plu verbi; mu uti: anti u-la, na kogita habe nuli klari semani, e plu verbi; na uti te expresi-auto, dona nuli klari semani a plu audi-pe.

    Kron intra prima-skola intra Brisbane, mi pa gene ge-doce 'parti e analisi', mi kogita: u-ci eqa reali gene-ski.  Sed intra seqe sko-tem, mi pa akusti nuli ma de u-ci valu-iso info.

    Ka plu doce-pe habe u krisi in mu logi de England-lingua, mu ne doce plu traditio regula de analisi: u-ci akti habe krimi.


    -----

    Intr an origi publiko de Glosa, Ron Clark pa deskribe plu VERBOID e plu AUXI VERBI.  Plu-ci koncepa ne habe simpli, e ko-co, ka posi konfusi, Ron ne kleisto mu intra _GLOSA 6000_ .  Sed, na a-nu uti plu -ci idea intra na uti de Glosa!

    Plu AUXI VERBI [AUXILIARY AMPLIFIERS] eqa tanto akti-verbi.  Mu ne habe solo uti, iso un AKTI VERBI, sed mu modifi u substantia verbi alo u VERBOID.

    EG  Tu pote (akti id).    Tu pote akti id.   An pote gene sko de id.

    LISTA.  debi, spe, hedo, more, nece, permito, feno, posi, pote, prepara, proto, tenta, dura, tende, volu     ex. _Glosa 1000_. 1984


    Plu VERBOID [VERBOIDS] eqa kompleti akti verbi, sed mu koncerne fo basi akti.  Per adi plu seqe modifi-face funktio-verbi, na pote krea u 'komplexi akti verbi' ko ge modifi semani.

  EG  ki apo [depart], dice akorda [agree], akti gira [rotate]

  Lista.  (ex _GLOSA 1000_, 1984)

akti

akusti

bali

dona

detekti

dice

eqa

este

face

gene

ge-habe

kine

moti

lose

reakti

stimula

tena

tira

vide


   E so, Ron pa fero plu-ci idea kon an ex "Interglossa",  sed pa decide: mu fu kausa tro de dificili.  U PUSI-AKTI-GREGA [VERB PHRASE] es u maxi dificili mero de Glosa, e mi prefero un uti de plu jugo marka [hyphens] to monstro u holo koncepa iso mo ra.

EG  U ju-an pa tenta vide-peri an, iso an pa gene ge-trakto in u kamera.


           | .... [VP] .....  |          | ..... [VP] .... |


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

with "du" although it was not quite orthodox. Other people have followed 
suite and I have found their glosa much, much easier to follow. However I

and future but nothing for present. As far as I see it, there is no or hardly 
any such thing as present tense - things start in the past and finish in the 
future so I wonder whether  "pafu", "pu", "fa"  might not be better. mi pafu 
nu ki a u viki - i am going to the village,  mi pafu itera ki.....  I go 
(repeatedly) ......

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe..... du pafu fa pu.........
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 2001 13:37:56 -0700

> [I believe that "du" is good for doing or continuing. I
> would prefer that we find a new word that indicates the
> verb start. Lojban has words for past, present, future, and
> verb-start.]

> [Perhaps we could use "avu" to mean verb-start? Otherwise,
> I will continue to use "du".]

Lojban is not a good model for a real language, nor is Esperanto;
I would not trust any feature that isn't found in a great number
of natural languages, preferably a majority.

Nearly all human languages do use TAM morphemes or auxiliaries
as Glosa does to flag verbs (and something similar for nouns).
But in Tok Pisin there *is* a particle "i" used to separate
the subject from predicate (verb); however, it is disappearing
from common use. It works something like this:

Subject i verb. (intransitive)
Subject i verb em object. (transitive)

There are plenty of useful particles listed at the bottom of
the basic Glosa reference page, and they work better than
the TP method to flag the verb phrase without redundancy.
Not just the TENSES, but also the MODALS and VERBOIDS:
http://www.zeta.org.au/~rgaskell/glosaref.htm

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe..... du pafu fa pu.........
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 2001 15:43:12 -0700

> [But Glosa is isolating and/or analytical, unlike most
> natural languages.]

It's not at all unusual, especially in Asia. More like 33%.
Languages tend to cycle through analytical/isolating to
agglutinating to inflectional. Egyptian went around the
loop several times.

> [So, you believe that all words in the sections: TENSES,
> MODALS, and VERBOIDS do always start a verb phrase? I don't
> believe that.]

Read through the Glosa samples for instances of auxiliaries
such as "sti" used to introduce a verb phrase. Using an
adverb-of-time isn't the only or necessarily best way.

> ["Pote" and "bali" can act as the nouns 'capability' and
> 'throw (n)'. Right? If not, I don't understand some basic
> rules of Glosa.]

With some difficulty anything can be verbed or nouned ...
"u bali = a throw", but without such a clear indication
(or override) "bali = to throw" retaining its verbiness.

========
To: Glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 2001 20:01:05 -0400 (EDT)

On Fri, 26 Oct 2001, Mikhail Adigeyev wrote (excerpt):

> I hope I could be of use as a Russian speaker...

    Yes, you probably can.  Most of the people here are native speakers
of English.  Welcome.

> And I have a question. Lurking through this list's archive I've noted
> that 'du' is used as a default verb marker. But in Glosa descripthions
> that I've met in the net it was said that 'du' means continous
> tense... Am I wrong?

    No, you are correct.  It is only some of the active users here 
who seem to think that they need to use a default verb marker.  Not
everyone agrees.  'Du' is actually a continuative/imperfective aspect
marker.  One of the design principles behind Glosa, if I have
understood them correctly, is that as a strictly isolating language 
it is not necessary to mark verbs for tense, aspect, or mood unless
a particular situation actually requires it -- nor nouns for number.

-- 
Paul Bartlett
bartlett{at}smart{dot}net

At 17:38 26/10/2001 +0400, you wrote:

>Hi!

>

>I'm a newcomer to this list and to Glosa in general.

>A brief info about myself:

>I'm an amatour in linguistics and am lurking through a few of

>lang-lists here (conlang, auxlang, glosalist and such).

>As to my knowing languages, I speak/read/write Russian (my native

>language), English, a bit of Esperanto and German. And now I'm rather

>impressed by Glosa :)

*   Welcome Mikail,

      Nice to hear of your response to Glosa.


>I hope I could be of use as a Russian speaker...

>

*     Yes it is true: the main linguistic question for me is, how does the syntax of Russian and Glosa match!?


>And I have a question. Lurking through this list's archive I've noted

>that 'du' is used as a default verb marker. But in Glosa descripthions

>that I've met in the net it was said that 'du' means continous

>tense... Am I wrong?

>

*   True   ~An bate mi.~   Present Tense; Neverending Tense; and also

                  more of the same tense that I have already established.


           ~An du bate mi.~  Present Continuous Tense: [He is continuing

                                                 to hit me.]


           ~An nu bate mi.~  'Nowy' Present Tense; better for forcing the

                  reader to acknowlege the start of a VERB PHRASE; but

                  still not necessary.


P.S. I have found these concepts handy:-

        'verb' =  substantive word functioning as a verb.

        VERB PHRASE  =  all between the Subject and Object.

        Sequential heirarchy in both VERB PHRASES and NOUN PHRASES, such

           that the least semantically significant words are at the start

           of a phrase, and the meaning becomes stronger as you progress

           through the phrase culminating in the major substantive word

           at the end of the phrase.  {Linguistic term: Head Final}


P.P.S.  If you have not already done so, I suggest that you read Seminars 1. and 2. in my website.  You might suggest matters to be raked over in future 'seminars.'


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

At 15:00 26/10/2001 EDT, you wrote: 

>>>>

<excerpt><fontfamily><param>arial</param><smaller>I found that I was
often not sure of the meaning of glosa sentences because ,mainly, of the
difficulty of pinning down the verb phrase. I experimented with "du"
although it was not quite orthodox. Other people have followed suite and
I have found their glosa much, much easier to follow. However I agree
that "du" is not really the best choice. We hav e"pa" and "fu" for past
and future but nothing for present. As far as I see it, there is no or
hardly any such thing as present tense - things start in the past and
finish in the future so I wonder whether  "pafu", "pu", "fa"  might not
be better. mi pafu nu ki a u viki - i am going to the village,  mi pafu
itera ki.....  I go (repeatedly) ...... </smaller> 

</fontfamily></excerpt><fontfamily><param>arial</param><<<<<<<<

Karo Syd,

    I do think that Glosa's Present Tense is very wide, and does not need
extra emphasis; only when wishing to specify a point in the present,
would it seem necessary to locate a ~nu~ at the start of the VERB
PHRASE.

   ~nu~ was added by Ron Clark after he sorted out the simple tense
particles, when he saw the need for pin-point a moment in time.

   The English tense construction "... will have ..." is given by the
Glosa construction, ~fu-pa~, and I am trying to recall any suggested
meaning for the ~pa-fu~ reverse construction, but can't.  However, I do
believe the hyphen is necessary to avoid the human speaker from sounding
it as one word, and also, for the mechanical reader (computer) to
recognise it as a construction of two words, and not as a neologism.


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

</fontfamily>

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe..... du pafu fa pu.........
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 12:46:01 +1000

At 14:43 26/10/2001 -0700, Kevin wrote:
>--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
>   .....
>> There are plenty of useful particles listed at the bottom
>> of the basic Glosa reference page, and they work better 
>> than the TP method to flag the verb phrase 
>
>So, tu du kredi; pan verba de plu klasi: "TENSES", "MODALS"
>e "VERBOIDS" du pan-tem proto u akti-verba frase? Mi du
>ne-kredi id.
>
>[So, you believe that all words in the sections: TENSES,
>MODALS, and VERBOIDS do always start a verb phrase? I don't
>believe that.]
>
>"Pote" e "bali" du pote akti homo plu nomina-verba,
>"capability" e "throw (n)". Veri? Si ne, mi du ne-logi plu
>basi lega de Glosa.
>
>["Pote" and "bali" can act as the nouns 'capability' and
>'throw (n)'. Right? If not, I don't understand some basic
>rules of Glosa.]
>
*  This is possible.
   Ron did go heavily on the punctuation within Glosa sentences, suggesting
that as syntax is all about structure, perhaps we could demark phrasal and
clausal boundaries with colons (:), semicolons (;) and commas (,).

   Maybe the humble comma can be, where necessary, a NP starter 'flag.'

   As I recall, the idea was to indicate the start of phrases, where
necessary for clarity, with a comma, and for the start of clauses, a
semicolon would indicate the greater complexity of the 'flagged'
syntactical unit.
  EG (using English)
   The boy; who was running up the hill, unfortunately broke his 
          ^                            ^ 
          |                            |
          Adjectival Clause 'flag'     (end of clause)/'flags' VERB PH
           

leg, as he fell.
   ^
   |
   'flag' for Indirect Object = NOUN PHRASE


    In this example, there was no need to flag the Object "his leg." 

   As I have said before, English is a 'bastard language' having both
syntactic grammar and inflections.  However, in it, the use of punctuation
has proved very handy over the years.  Have a look at you favourite
novelist's work: select an action scene, and read analytically to see how
many commas and semi-colons have been used.  In the excitement of reading,
these punctuation marks did not impinge on your consciousness; but, they
certainly helped by ensuring that you got the full meaning of the action
scene, and rapidly so.
  The same applies to Glosa, which has the syntactic grammar of English,
but lacks its inflections.  Probably, the use of punctuation is even more
important in Glosa than it is in English.
  A difference, here: you have not had the experience of reading action
scenes in Glosa novels.  This suggests that there is a need for Glosa-pe to
write some racy short stories in the language.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell
  P.S. In my attempt to abstract the syntactic structures from the words
(GAS System), I used single spaces for gaps between symbols representing
words, double spaces between the symbols of different phrases, and triple
spaces for the gaps separating clauses.  I did not show any punctuation.
So, I suggest that there are creative ways around all problems: perhaps the
Glosa-pe can agree on the most suitable, and possibly most creative, way of
'flagging' phrases and clauses.  R.G.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 13:04:01 +1000

At 20:01 26/10/2001 -0400, Paul Bartlett wrote:
>On Fri, 26 Oct 2001, Mikhail Adigeyev wrote (excerpt):
>   .....
>> And I have a question. Lurking through this list's archive I've noted
>> that 'du' is used as a default verb marker. But in Glosa descripthions
>> that I've met in the net it was said that 'du' means continous
>> tense... Am I wrong?
>
>    No, you are correct.  It is only some of the active users here 
>who seem to think that they need to use a default verb marker.  Not
>everyone agrees.  'Du' is actually a continuative/imperfective aspect
>marker.  One of the design principles behind Glosa, if I have
>understood them correctly, is that as a strictly isolating language 
>it is not necessary to mark verbs for tense, aspect, or mood unless
>a particular situation actually requires it -- nor nouns for number.
>
*   This is the rule stated very clearly.

    However, what IS missing from Glosa because of this 'lean' structure is
the idea of "redundancy."  If a Glosa message could get garble through an
imperfect communication channel, then it is very possible that some element
of redundancy would be introduced.

EG (English)   a boy/ the boys   .   goes/ went; did go; was going

   (Glosa)     u ju-an/ plu ju-an   .   ki/ pa ki; pa du ki

   Obviously, redundancy has come into English for a good reason; and, the
need for it can perhaps be discovered in Glosa: possibly there is no such
thing as a perfect communication channel, but the Glosa authors simply
assumed that it would be there.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

Subject: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe..... du pafu fa pu.........
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri, 26 Oct 2001 20:50:20 -0700

> Agreed (in principle). But where is the unambiguous list of
> words that indicate the start of a verb-phrase?

Hasn't been made, only implied. The categorizations done
by the authors for the glosaref and glosa-classified
are helpful but not entirely satisfying.

> Forgive my bad attitude (I haven't gotten enough sleep this
> week), but I thought one of the key features of Glosa was
> that any non-particle word could take on any part of
> speech.

I haven't noticed any lack of politeness or reasonableness.
Indeed, the authors have attempted to erase the differences
between nouns, adjectives, and verbs. But the only way it
seems possible is to regard the 200-or-so grammatical particles
as a class of "clitic" morphemes, that coerce the open class
of "generic roots" into their proper grammatical functions.
Like little tugboats or something.

Hello Robin,

Saturday, October 27, 2001, 5:29:55 AM, you wrote:


>>I hope I could be of use as a Russian speaker...

>>

RG> *     Yes it is true: the main linguistic question for me is, how does
RG> the syntax of Russian and Glosa match!?

They don't :-) Russian is heavily inflecting language, with free word
order. Thus, it's an 'antipode' for Glosa.

>>And I have a question. Lurking through this list's archive I've noted

>>that 'du' is used as a default verb marker. But in Glosa descripthions

>>that I've met in the net it was said that 'du' means continous

>>tense... Am I wrong?

>>

RG> P.P.S.  If you have not already done so, I suggest that you read Seminars 1. and 2.
RG> in my website.  You might suggest matters to be raked over in future 'seminars.'
Thank you for your explanations, Robin. I will surely visite your
site.

There are some features in Glosa that I consider, eh, not so nice.
If you wouldn't consider if too saucy, I'll tell what thay are:
1) 'x' makes spelling irregular. This letter makes Glosa words
recognizable by people, who know Latin or Romance languages (or
English, which accepted Latin roots without transformation). But for
future generations (when Glosa will be accepted as IAL :)))) ) this
will be just an odd feature...
2) 'q': well, I dont know exactly, how many languages have this sound
claster? Russian doesn't. This sound was included in Glosa just for
simplifing Latin roots' recognizability, wasn't it?
3) I have troubles trying to pronounce words with vowel clusters,
especially in the end of the word. Why not use, say 'qesti' instead of
'questio'?
4) I think, that forming imperative by using bare verb is absolutely
euro-centric... Do other languages do this? Why not use special
particle for this purpose?

One more thing with pjonology: as far as I know, both chinese and
japanese languages don't distinguish 'l' and 'r' (chinese has only 'l'
and japanese has only 'r'). I think, chinese and japanese speakers
will have troubles speaking Glosa...

And I have a question about stress. Some sources say that penultimate
syllable should be stressed, others advise to stress the vowel before
the last consonant? Where should I stress the word 'quesio'?

RG> Saluta,
RG> Robin Gaskell

Thanks for answers.

Saluta,
Mikhail

Interesting example. I would want to say something like
~kani maxi-ami pe~, which would be "dog(s) maximum-friend

a need to insert some verb-start indicator.

  uno-kani pan-tem maxi-ami a pe
  any dog(s) always best-friend to person(s)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Questions about Glosa (was: Mi es u neo-pe)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 08:07:20 -0700 (PDT)

--- Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru> wrote:
> They don't :-) Russian is heavily inflecting language,
> with free word order. Thus, it's an 'antipode' for Glosa.

Do you find Glosa to be easy and fun? Or strange and
difficult?

> There are some features in Glosa that I consider, eh, not
> so nice. If you wouldn't consider if too saucy, I'll tell
what
> thay are:

Grati! I'll respond, and hopefully be in line with the
official answers.

> 1) 'x' makes spelling irregular. This letter makes Glosa
> words recognizable by people, who know Latin or Romance
> languages (or English, which accepted Latin roots without
> transformation). But for future generations (when Glosa
will 
> be accepted as IAL :)))) ) this will be just an odd
feature...

This certainly would be easier if we changed 'x' to 'ks':
  ~akexi~ -> ~aleksi~ 
And for words starting with 'x', change to 'z':
  ~xeno~ -> ~zeno~
(since I have a hard time pronouncing it as "kseno" or
"kzeno" anyway)

Personally, I would be willing to give up the visual
recognition of 'x', since it's not used that much. But it
would be a significant change to the language. 

Is there any support for allowing alternate spellings? I
don't think I would like that option, because usually I
feel that options in a language make it _more_ difficult.

> 2) 'q': well, I dont know exactly, how many languages
> have this sound claster? Russian doesn't. This sound was 
> included in Glosa just for simplifing Latin roots'
recognizability, 
> wasn't it?

Probably. Again, it would be easier if we changed 'q' to
'kw':
  ~quadra~ -> ~kwadra~

Or we could just drop the 'w' sound entirely, and either
pronounce 'q' and 'k' the same way, or spell everything
with 'k'. But we would hit several major conflicts:
  ~qo~ -> ~ko~ !!!

Again, I'm not that fond of 'q', and it's not used that
much, so I wouldn't mind switching to 'kw'. But again, it
would be a big move for the language. And, again, I would
probably be opposed to making it an option.

> 3) I have troubles trying to pronounce words with vowel
> clusters, especially in the end of the word. Why not use,
say
> 'qesti' instead of 'questio'?

Agreed. There aren't too many of these, but we should see
if we can gradually shift to the easier versions, like the
one you mentioned.

> 4) I think, that forming imperative by using bare verb is
> absolutely euro-centric... Do other languages do this?
Why not 
> use special particle for this purpose?

Interesting. It certainly is common among Auglangs. But,
then, most of them are Euro-clones anyway! I assume Russion
probably inflects the verb. Glosa would probably use a
particle to indicate an imperative, similar to 'qe' for
questions.

I have noticed a bit of ambiguity, because the first thing
may be a verb. To be consistent, I should at least stick a
verb marker at the front:
  Du lava  tu    manu
  Wash    your hands

But it is still ambiguous sometimes, as in:
   Pluvi.
   (It is) raining <or>
   (Please) rain

> And I have a question about stress. Some sources say that
> penultimate syllable should be stressed, others advise to

> stress the vowel before the last consonant? Where should
I 
> stress the word 'quesio'?

Vowels that appear together are treated as a single
syllable, 
so those two rules are saying the same thing. It would be:
  ~QEST-io~

Thank you again for bringing up some interesting
discussions.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Sense organ questions
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 08:48:00 -0700 (PDT)

mi du audi ko mi oti,
mi du vide (alo skope) ko mi oku, SED
mi du nasa ko mi nasa

Is there a good reason to have (or not have) different
words for the organ and the action? Would it be beter to
have:

mi du audi ko mi audi ALO
mi du oti ko mi oti ALO
mi du audi ko me audi-ra

Or do we need different words for "nose" and "smell (v)"?
We have ~rino~, so should ~nasa~ be "smell (v)"?

Also, is there really a difference between ~skope~ (look)
and ~vide~ (see)? In English, it's idiomatic that seeing is
more like being aware of something, whereas looking is more
passive. But how would you describe the difference(s) these
two words to a non-English speaker?

Kevin

Subject: [glosalist] programa
From: "Alexander E. Kirpichev" <brickswall{at}mtu-net.ru>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 20:38:17 +0400

Mi pa face u textu pro mi porgrama in England-lingua:
This is the message in Glosa-language. You can easy understand it by using
only small vocabulary. Glosa has no parts of speech, and use
Subject-Verb-Object verb order. It means that firstly we write a thing, who
is doer of action, then we write verb and after that a thing, who is
changhed/maked/done etc. my the action. E.g. Mi vide u feli. Mi= I,
vide=see, u = one, feli = cat. I see one cat. For further information see
glosa.org/apageaboutprogramifmarcelwillpermitmeputthere.

Qe vi pose korekti u textu e traduce id a plu hetero lingua?

Saluta,
Alex.
P.S. Now I'm writing a letter about me, like Syd and Kevin did.

  ~fu pa~ = shall have

Note: these are not hyphenated.

> It means that firstly we write a thing, who is doer of
action, 
> then we write verb and after that a thing, who is

"Which" would probably be better than "who", here, and
instead of doer you could say simply "does". I assume you
meant to type "changed". "Maked" isn't a word...perhaps
"created". "Affected" would be a great word, here. So this
could become:

It means that first we write a thing, which does an action,
then we write a verb and after that another thing, which is
affected/changed/created by the action.

cat. 

Actually, ~u~ is "a" or "the", as opposed to ~mo~ which is
"one".

This would all be so much easier if English weren't part of
our world!

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Hard to pronounce (was: Questions about Glosa)
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 12:13:56 -0700

Kevin wrote:

> I should mention that there are a several letter
> combinations in Glosa that I have a fair amount of trouble
> pronouncing:

I agree, and Glosa's phonology could benefit from a revision.
The more letters removed, the better (easier pronunciation),
and worse (less visual and aural recognizability).

> ~excepti, excepti, excite~

It's CEPTI=accept, and EXCEPTI=except. The pattern is obvious.
Latin EX is Greek EK, so that's one possible fix for this case.
There are thousands of internationally recognized words using
the pattern of Latin EX, AD, CON, RE, PRE, and etc, as prefixes.

The first design choice is whether to allow them in Glosa or not,
and the authors have chosen the affirmative. But then they also
chose to spell "phonetically", which means that the boundaries of
pure compounds will undergo certain unfortunate changes (sandhi).

Imagine actually pronouncing "eks-chepti" or "eks-chludi"!
It's enough of a mess to demand a reform -- EKSEPTI that
no solution is going to please anybody.

> ~equs~

Q should be eliminated by KU, I think. X should be KS.
Remove at least QWXY and maybe HZ.

> ~frustra~ (not quite sure why)
> ~gnata~

Bad consonant clusters, GN and STR. For easy and consistent
spelling and pronunciation, all CCs must be removed. But,
most Latin/Greek roots would change beyond recognition.
Perhaps a careful selection could turn up enough roots
of the simple CVCV variety for a useful vocabulary?

> ~hiao~
> ~leuko~
> ~meio~

Diphthongs are just as bad as consonant clusters.
Maybe just a few like AU and AI would be OK?

> ~ritmo~

Perhaps clusters are acceptable when separated by
syllabic boundaries, like RIT-MO. Which leads to
other questions about terminal vowels etc.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Hard to pronounce (was: Questions about Glosa)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 13:35:43 -0700 (PDT)

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> Imagine actually pronouncing "eks-chepti" or
"eks-chludi"!

In my own core list, I've eliminated most of the "exc..."
words:
excele -> fo-boni
excepti -> exklude (not great, but better)
excesi -> plus
excite (still there so far)

> It's enough of a mess to demand a reform -- EKSEPTI that
> no solution is going to please anybody.

This is a good example of where I am willing to let the
language evolve over time, rather than attempt a big
reform. The drawback is that existing material with 'x'
would become obsolete, and the longer we wait, the more
existing material there will be. 

But that approach has worked well for natural languages, so
why not use it for an IAL as well? (Or, at least it has
"worked". Whether it has worked "well" is a matter of
opinion.)

> > ~hiao~
> > ~leuko~
> > ~meio~
> 
> Diphthongs are just as bad as consonant clusters.
> Maybe just a few like AU and AI would be OK?

Generally two-vowel diphthongs are ok for me (of course,
I'm used to them in English). Those three-vowel
combinations are really hard. I guess they would be
something like:
  hiao = "h-yeow"
  meio = "mayo"

Of course, I've removed meio from my list, by combining it
with mei, and hiao is basic, not core.

Kevin

On Sat, 27 Oct 2001, Kevin Smith wrote (excerpt):

> Actually, ~u~ is "a" or "the", as opposed to ~mo~ which is
> "one".

    It is confusing, both in the original Glosa documents and
elsewhere, to refer to 'u' as meaning 'a' and/or 'the'.  In other
words, the same little word could mean either definite or indefinite! 
Several years ago, in a paper mail, Wendy Ashby agreed that 'u' is
really a noun marker rather than the confusing 'a/the'.  (The same
holds, obviously, with 'plu'.)

bibli epi u mensa~ ???

> > "one".

Paul O. Bartlett  wrote

>     It is confusing, both in the original Glosa documents and
> elsewhere, to refer to 'u' as meaning 'a' and/or 'the'.  In other
> words, the same little word could mean either definite or indefinite! 
> Several years ago, in a paper mail, Wendy Ashby agreed that 'u' is
> really a noun marker rather than the confusing 'a/the'.  (The same
> holds, obviously, with 'plu'.)
> 

I agree, they are noun markers. 

But in practice this also means that they correspond to 
"a/the" in English. The confusion this may cause is not 
general. For example, Russian manages to get by without
this definite/indefinite distinction!


And speaking of Russian...
I too would like to welcome Mikhail Adigeyev to the list.
It's great to have people on the list whose native language
is not English, like Alexander, Marcel, Kjell  and Henry. 
Contributions from their perspective are very helpful to 
getting a view of Glosa free from the distortions caused 
by looking through "English spectacles".

Saluta,
Nick

From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>

Kevin Smith wrote
>
> mi du audi ko mi oti,
> mi du vide (alo skope) ko mi oku, SED
> mi du nasa ko mi nasa
> 
~Mi olface per mi nasa.~

The Glosa word lists give the verb meaning for part 
of the body "X" as "to use X" ~akti per X~.

~oku~ = peep
~nasa~ = smell or sniff
~oti~ =  'bug'
~pedi~ = kick
~pugna~ = punch
~denti~ = grin
~glosa~ = lick
~kubita~ = nudge
~palma~ = slap
~manu~ = pass/hand over or smack
~beka~ = peck
~peri-braki~ = hug

> Also, is there really a difference between ~skope~ (look)
> and ~vide~ (see)? 

For me, there is not a significant difference in meaning.
I would say it relates to degree of attention paid to the
visual sensations.

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Where does the verb start?
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sun, 28 Oct 2001 00:21:22 +0100

Robin Gaskell wrote
> Intr an origi publiko de Glosa, Ron Clark pa deskribe plu VERBOID 
> e plu AUXI VERBI. Plu-ci koncepa ne habe simpli, e ko-co, ka posi 
> konfusi, Ron ne kleisto mu intra _GLOSA 6000_ . Sed, na a-nu uti 
> plu -ci idea intra na uti de Glosa!
> Plu AUXI VERBI [AUXILIARY AMPLIFIERS] eqa tanto akti-verbi. 
> Mu ne habe solo uti, iso un AKTI VERBI, sed mu modifi u substantia
> verbi alo u VERBOID.
> EG Tu pote (akti id). Tu pote akti id. An pote gene sko de id.
>LISTA. debi, spe, hedo, more, nece, permito, feno, posi, pote, 
> prepara, proto, tenta, dura, tende, volu ex. _Glosa 1000_. 1984

Central Glosa 1993
15 helper verbs
debi, pa debi, hedo, more, nece, lice, feno, fu posi, pote,
prepara, proto, tenta, dura, tende, volu.


> Plu VERBOID [VERBOIDS] eqa kompleti akti verbi, 
> sed mu koncerne fo basi akti. Per adi plu seqe modifi-face 
> funktio-verbi, na pote krea u 'komplexi akti verbi' ko ge 
> modifi semani. EG ki apo [depart], dice akorda [agree], 
> akti gira [rotate]
> Lista. (ex _GLOSA 1000_, 1984)
> akti  akusti  bali  dona  detekti  dice  eqa  este  face
> gene ge-habe kine moti lose reakti stimula tena tira vide

Central Glosa 1993
20 verboids
akti, akusti, apo, bali, dona, detekti, dice, eqa, este, face,
gene, habe, ki, lose, moti, reakti, sti, tena, trakto, vide

"These are 20 very interesting verbs, because, amazingly,
they play a part in the action of all the many thousands of
other verbs. They are very useful as they will provide
precision when there is a possibility of
mis-understanding." Central Glosa 1993

Saluta,
Nick

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Word List Progress (and a question)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 27 Oct 2001 18:11:05 -0700 (PDT)

I'm getting fairly close to a first release of my Glosa
word list. I've "promoted" about thirty words from "basic"
to "core". I have not had to invent any new words, nor
import any non-basic GID words. 

I've replaced quite a few long imported words with compound
words made up of core roots, and simply omitted quite a few
words because they are redundant or not truly core words.

Anyway, one of the biggest single changes I've made
involved several words. I'd like to get the group's
feedback on whether I've made any bad decisions in this
area. I'm certainly willing to adjust my definitions as
needed.

Here are my current entries:

* insignia: Removed from the list
* makula: spot (stain), stain (spot), mark (stain), blot
(stain), smear
* marka: mark (blemish), blemish, scratch, nick
* nomina-signi: signature (newly added)
* sema: sign (n), signal, gesture, beckon, token
* signatura: Removed from list
* signi: sign (brand), mark (brand), badge, brand, symbol
* signi-te: flag, banner, pennant (newly added)

Do you agree that they "hold together" as a group, and
follow the "one concept=one word" philosophy? Are they
reasonably compatible with the existing intended (GID)
meanings?

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Sense organ questions
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 28 Oct 2001 07:48:26 -0800 (PST)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> The Glosa word lists give the verb meaning for part 
> of the body "X" as "to use X" ~akti per X~.
> 
> ~oku~ = peep
> ~nasa~ = smell or sniff
> ~oti~ =  'bug'
> ~pedi~ = kick
> ~pugna~ = punch
> ~denti~ = grin
> ~glosa~ = lick
> ~kubita~ = nudge
> ~palma~ = slap
> ~manu~ = pass/hand over or smack
> ~beka~ = peck
> ~peri-braki~ = hug

Well, ~oti~ = 'bug' just makes no sense at all. I'm not
putting that in my core list.

For several of the others, it seems really weird to me to
have the word for a body part have a very specific, often
rare, meaning as a verb. Why would ~oku~ be "peep", and not
"see"? Why would ~denti~ be "grin", and not "bite"?

~pedi~ could be "step on". ~pugna~ could be "pound",
~kubita~ and ~palma~ seem ok, but somewhat idiomatic.

Maybe it would be better to disallow using body parts as
verbs. Or perhaps only use them in completely unambiguous,
non-idiomatic cases: eyes see, ears hear, noses smell, and
tongues lick.

I don't know what to do.

Kevin

far is:

~pulsa~ push, thrust, shove...
~itera-pulsa~ pulse, pulsate, throb...

Any other ideas?

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Words that I removed
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 28 Oct 2001 12:59:16 -0800 (PST)

In case anyone is curious, I have a list of words that I
removed from my word list before publishing it. That's
about 650 words (so far). Words were removed for several
possible reasons, which are tagged as follows:

1. Another word was better for that meaning
(x-core->newword)
2. The word was too rare to be in core (x-core-rare)
3. The word wasn't in core to begin with (x-basic)
4. The word didn't have clear Glosa roots (x-core-noroot)
5. The meaning of the word was unclear (x-core-unclear)
6. The compound word was obvious and unhelpful
(x-core-nohelp)

I think that's all the reasons. Note that in many cases I
split a word into two meanings, and then marked one of the
meanings as being removed. For example, ~porta~ was
retained as doorway, but the meanings of "port", "haven",
and "carry" were removed.

Anyway, the page is up in a temporary location, so if you
are really interested in it I suggest you download a local
copy. Also note that I'm not done removing words yet, so
this list will continue to grow. Currently, it is here:

  http://www.qualitycode.com/glosa/removed.txt

Eventually, I'll remove it. Assuming I have time, I hope to
incorporate all this information into a detailed document
describing all my word list changes (adds, omits, etc.).
For now, if you're really curious where your favorite word
went, this should answer it.

Kevin

From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Sun, 28 Oct 2001 17:50:50 -0500 (EST)

On Sat, 27 Oct 2001, Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:

> > It is confusing, both in the original Glosa documents and
> > elsewhere, to refer to 'u' as meaning 'a' and/or 'the'.  In other
> > words, the same little word could mean either definite or indefinite!
> 
> How to translate these examples?
> 
> Austin is *a* city
> Austin is *the* capital
> Austin is beautiful

Austin es civita.
Austin es kapitala.
Austin es kali.

    Why is it necessary to use any kind of article at all?  Latin
managed for a millenium and a half without articles.  Russian friends,
am I right that modern Russian has no articles?  Those of us whose
native languages use articles are sometimes puzzled that a language 
can function without them.  My own opinion is that an international
auxiliary language is better off without articles at all.  Even 
languages that have articles do not use them in the same way, as can
readily be ascertained when a non-native speaker uses articles in ways
that sound odd to native speakers.

-- 
Paul Bartlett
bartlett{at}smart{dot}net

From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com

> > How to translate these examples?
> >
> > Austin is *a* city
> > Austin is *the* capital
> > Austin is beautiful
>
> Austin es civita.
> Austin es kapitala.
> Austin es kali.

No, that loses the significant differences.

> Why is it necessary to use any kind of article at all?

You can replace the function of articles, *but*
you shouldn't just ignore or forget those functions.
Others will say you can delete the copula, as in Russian.
Sure, but you still need to be able to translate the
ideas accurately, from any language. Baby, bathwater.

To: "Paul O. Bartlett" <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>

From: "Mikhail Adigeyev" <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Date: Mon, 29 Oct 2001 09:03:29 +0300 (MSK)

Saluta,

Monday, October 29, 2001, 1:50:50 AM, you wrote:

POB> On Sat, 27 Oct 2001, Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:

POB>     Why is it necessary to use any kind of article at all?  Latin
POB> managed for a millenium and a half without articles.  Russian friends,
POB> am I right that modern Russian has no articles?
Yes, you are right. As far as I know, Russian never has articles at
all. We use words like 'that' to explicitely show that the object was
mentioned before.
I think, special particles could be more flexible and useful to
express definiteness of objects.

And if we introduce compulsory definite/indefinite atricles, we have
to state a number of rules of using them in cases which are not
evident (like geographical names, organizations, matters, etc). This
will complicate language a good deal, IMHO.

Saluta,
Mikhail

I recently wondered if we could use ~u-co~ instead of ~id~.
Do you agree? Do you like or dislike [the idea]?

Kevin

P.S. ~u-co~ means "the just-said (used as a
subject/object)", which seems (to me) to be what ~id~ also
refers to. I'm no language expert, so I could be way off
here. It just seemed like an interesting way to reuse part
of the language, increasing consistency.

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, <kevins{at}pobox{dot}com>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] ~id~ vice ~u-co~
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Tue, 30 Oct 2001 20:20:25 -0000

~-co~ refers to the whole of the previous sentence or paragraph,
while ~id~ usually refers to just a previously mentioned noun.

~Id~ also acts as an empty pronoun, with no referent as in:
~Id ne pluvi.~ = "It's not raining."
to distinguish the sentence from
~Ne pluvi.~ = "Don't rain!"

Saluta,
Nick

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] programa
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Wed, 31 Oct 2001 06:14:28 -0800

> > > Austin is *a* city
> > > Austin is *the* capital
> > > Austin is beautiful

> Can you explain what the significant differences are?

The indefinite article means "one of possibly many,
and one which has not been discussed recently".
You can figure out the definite and the adjectival uses.

In languages without articles, these distinctions must
be marked on the verb or idiomatically or with the
addition of many more words.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Wed, 31 Oct 2001 08:21:17 -0800

Alex wrote:

> Russian had articles in ancient times, but after evolution the language
> need np articles any more.

Some people have article-phobia and I don't wish to scare them,
but there is a whole set of words usually called "determiners"
to handle all the necessary functions in Glosa. One of those
functions is to show where noun phrases begin, just as tense
particles and auxiliaries mark the start of the verb phrase
(mainly, the direct object; other cases have prepositions).

I question the adequacy of the set of determiners in Glosa,
because it seems nobody can adequately translate the 3 most
common uses of English articles. Of course it could be easily
fixed by adding a particle or two if lacking. All natural
languages have some means of expressing these things ...

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] programa
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Wed, 31 Oct 2001 08:43:30 -0800 (PST)

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> > > > Austin is *a* city
> > > > Austin is *the* capital
> > > > Austin is beautiful
> 
> > Can you explain what the significant differences are?
> 
> The indefinite article means "one of possibly many,
> and one which has not been discussed recently".
> You can figure out the definite and the adjectival uses.
> 
> In languages without articles, these distinctions must
> be marked on the verb or idiomatically or with the
> addition of many more words.

I haven't really formed a strong opinion, so I'd like to
understand this further.

When we say "Austin is *the* capital", we really mean that
it is the capital _of Texas_. There are certainly many
capitals in the world, so if we leave Texas out of the
sentence, it is actually more accurate to say that Austin
is *a* capital (~Austin es krati-urba~).

Now, if we really do want to say that Austin is *the*
capital of Texas, how do we say that in Glosa? ~Austin es
mo krati-urba de Texas~? That makes it sound like it's one
of many. ~Austin es u solo krati-urba de Texas~? I guess
that works. It does require two extra words. 

I don't think we could say ~Austin es solo krati-urba de
Texas~ because that would mean (to me) that Austin is
merely the capital of Texas.

For the third, ~Austin es kali~ or ~Austin du kali~ works
for me. 

Kevin

plu soni de vokali


te face plu-co soni ma ........... , mi du sugesti ke na du uti plu mensura. 
tem u face de vokali, plu gono de u stoma ki a u certa horizo loka, plu ana e 
sub labia du sume u vertiko mo e u glosa u hetero mo, kognati ko u guma. tem 
na du hiao tri-co mero du aperi a maxi si komodi.
plu stoma gono 40mm aperi, plu labia 36mm aperi e glosa/guma 100%. plu-co 
pote es plu norma loka.

na pote face u tabula homo u-ci:-
"a" plu gono   45mm alo 90% de u haio
    plu labia  25mm
    glosa/guma 90%
"e" g   45mm
    l   14mm
    g/g 50%
"i" g   50mm
    l   5mm
    gg  10%  
"o" g   30mm 
    l   20mm 
    gg  60%
"u" g   10mm 
    l   7mm 
    gg  7%
u no-klari, neutra soni     g 40mm 
    l   10mm 
    gg  10%

...................................................
vowel sounds

to make more reliable the pronouncing of glosa vowels, i suggest using 
measurements. when making a vowel, the corners of the mouth take up a certain 
horizontal position, the upper and lower lips a vertical one and the tongue 
yet another in relation to the palate. when we yawn these three parts open as 
widely as is comfortable - corners= 40    apart , lips= 36      apart and 
tongue/palate= 100%     apart. these could be looked upon as standard 
positions.
we could show the vowels as:-
measurements in millimeters
a corners=45      or     90  % of a yawn
  lips=25
  tongue/palate=90%
e corners 45         
  lips 14
  t/p 50%
i c 50  
  l 5
  tp 10%
o c 30  
  l 20
  tp 60%
u c 10  
  l 7
  tp 7%
and a vague, neutral vowel, schwa
  c 40
  l 10
  tp  10%

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Subject: Re: Re[2]: [glosalist] Mi es u neo-pe
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 31 Oct 2001 21:13:39 -0000

Mikhail Adigeyev 
> 1) 'x' makes spelling irregular. This letter makes Glosa words
> recognizable by people, who know Latin or Romance languages (or
> English, which accepted Latin roots without transformation). But for
> future generations (when Glosa will be accepted as IAL :)))) ) this
> will be just an odd feature...

A nice possibility, besides recognisability, is that you can pronounce
it KS or KZ without having to alter the spelling of words. Also
pronouncing it Z is allowed when it occurs as the first letter in a
word for example ~xeno~.

> 2) 'q': well, I dont know exactly, how many languages have this sound
> claster? Russian doesn't. This sound was included in Glosa just for
> simplifing Latin roots' recognizability, wasn't it?

This also has possibilities for variations in pronunciation, KW or
KU. I think that even pronouncing it KV, would not make words
unintelligible.

> 3) I have troubles trying to pronounce words with vowel clusters,
> especially in the end of the word. Why not use, say 'qesti' instead of
> 'questio'?

The shortening of words in Glosa, is still an on-going process. So,
such a change would be in line with what the authors have been
doing up till now.

> 4) I think, that forming imperative by using bare verb is absolutely
> euro-centric... Do other languages do this? Why not use special
> particle for this purpose?

I believe that Mandarin uses a would meaning "invite" for this purpose.
Also I think, Hogben proposed in Interglossa that ~petitio~ (Glosa for
"invite") should be used. But I may have mis-remember that.

~Sti u lampa.~
~Place sti u lampa.~
~Petitio; tu sti u lampa.~

> One more thing with pjonology: as far as I know, both chinese and
> japanese languages don't distinguish 'l' and 'r' (chinese has only 'l'
> and japanese has only 'r'). I think, chinese and japanese speakers
> will have troubles speaking Glosa...

If you eliminate all sounds that are hard for people to pronounce, you
end up with only about 10 consonants and 3 vowels. I think it's better 
to tolerate some variation from a standard model pronunciation, 
than to end up with all your words looking and sounding very similar 
to each other.

Saluta,
Nick

> ~oti~

Thanks, yes. A couple days ago I realized what use of 'bug'
was intended. And I did end up including it in my list. In
fact, each body part lists whatever actions I could think
of. Examples:

digi: finger, toe, press (use finger), tap (use finger)

oti: ear, bug (eavesdrop), eavesdrop (use ears), hear (use
ears), listen (use ears)

pedi: foot, paw, kick (use foot), tread (use foot), stomp
(use foot), step on (use foot)

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] programa
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Wed, 31 Oct 2001 16:04:29 -0800

> Now, if we really do want to say that Austin is *the*
> capital of Texas, how do we say that in Glosa? ~Austin es
> mo krati-urba de Texas~? That makes it sound like it's one
> of many. ~Austin es u solo krati-urba de Texas~? I guess
> that works. It does require two extra words.

That's what I was talking about. There's no graceful translation.

> I don't think we could say ~Austin es solo krati-urba de
> Texas~ because that would mean (to me) that Austin is
> merely the capital of Texas.

It also doesn't work to say "this" (demonstrative) to simulate
a true "the" (definite). Oddly, Glosa has both "u/un" and "mo"
but still lacks any true articles. Using "u/un = the" and
"mo = a/an" is much too ugly ...

> For the third, ~Austin es kali~ or ~Austin du kali~ works
> for me.

Me too, and it illustrates something about terminology.
In many languages there are no adjectives, and "es kali"
would function as an intransitive verb. The particle
"es = is" isn't really even a verb in English, it's more
of an auxiliary or "copula". Another way to say it in
Glosa might be "Austin habe kali = Austin has beauty",
but again the putative verb is more of an auxiliary.
It gets even worse if "Austin kali" (deletion of the
copula) is allowed ...

The pattern that I think represents the True Spirit of Glosa
is something like this because it can't get much simpler
(optional stuff in parentheses, and ignoring pronouns/etc):

noun-phrase = (preposition +) determiner + (adjective +) noun

verb-phrase = (tense +) auxiliary + verb

intransitive-sentence = noun-phrase + verb-phrase

transitive-sentence = noun-phrase + verb-phrase + noun-phrase
